loss reduction component of the proposed project is to reduce technical energy losses to seven percent by
1998.
Hydropower Suppl Constraint
3.12   Since July 1992, TANESCO has practiced severe peak load shedding due to drought in the
Southern Africa region. As a result of the lower than average water inflows during 1991 and 1992, the
water level of the hydro reservoirs has declined significantly, resulting in reduced generation capacity in
the hydro plants both in the Great Ruaha and the Pangani River systems (para 4.2). Table 3.2 shows the
rated capacity, available capacity and reduced capacity of each major hydro plant. TANESCO's very
limited capacity reserves are exacerbating the shortfall fiurther because the company has not been able to
secure financing for the rehabilitation of all the diesel units as originally planned. As a result TANESCO
has been unable to compensate sufficiently, with diesel capacity for the reduced hydro capacity of 114
MW. Peak load shedding has at times been more than 100 MW, but TANESCO has tried to reduce the
energy shortfall as much as possible. In fact, to avoid even greater load shedding, TANESCO in the past
operated the hydroplant at very high plant factors which ultimately led to low reservoir levels.
TANESCO expressed its concern to IDA about the low water levels and IDA responded with a mission to
examine hydro plant use and the status of the reservoirs. As a result of this mission, TANESCO and
IDA agreed on a strict rule curve to govern hydro plant operations and avoid a major shutdown of the
system before the rainy season. However, the water level at the Mtera reservoir has been rapidly
decreasing, is currently close to its minimum operating level, and probably will not completely fill up
even if the rainfall in the next rainy season is at its normal level. Thus, TANESCO's shortage of
capacity is likely to continue through 1994.

the low-growth scenario, the average annual increase in generation would be 4.1 percent, with a related
average increase of 4.3 percent in maximum demand. Table 3.3 summarizes the base case forecast and

3.14 IDA's forecast is a revision of the projections which TANESCO prepared as part of its 1990
Power Sector Update Study. This revision consists of adjustments IDA has made to take account of
actual 1991 generation, actual and projected tariff rate increases, and potential increases in the efficiency
of industial electricity use. Additionally, IDA has made a change in the forecast's estimated technical
and non-technical losses to conform to the expected impact of proposed project components to help reduce
system losses. The forecast assumes that some of the electricity going to non-technical losses at present
will become sales.
3.15   The base forecast assumes an average annual GDP growth rate of about 4 percent during 1992-
2002. For industrial demand estimation, the forecast uses a declining elasticity of electricity demand to
GDP, which the forecast assumes wil take place because of higher capacity utilization and the use of
more energy-efficient technology. For residential demand, the forecast considers the number of new



- 30-
connections expcted and an estimated average electricity conumption per household. The forecast bases
the munber of new connections on those that have taken place In recent years and an esthnated average
anmual increase of 4 percent in the number of urban lhouseholds. For the average electricity consumption
per connection, the forecast uses historical data buw assumes a decrease in the average consunption per
household, which should result from consumer response to increasing tariffs and the increasing proportion
of lower-income households in the network as it expands.
3.16   According to the base case demand forecast, the system peak will increase from 297 MW in 1991
to 565 MW in 2002. The corresponding generation requirement will rise from 1,739 GWh in 1991 to
3,291 GWh in 2002. While this represents a faster growth rate than in the early 1980s, it is less than the
growth over the past five years. The projected generation requirement also assmes a substantial
reduction in both technical and non-technical losses, from about 21 percent at present to around 9 percent
in 2002. Under the base case, there will be a need for substantial new generation during the 1990's. The
rehabilitadon of the existing diesel units wiUl provide additional thennal capacity, and both the
redevelopment of the Pangani Falls station and the commissioning of the Lower Kihansi scheme will
provide new hydro capacity. However, despite these additions, the installation of new intermediate
capacity will be necessary before the conmmissioning of the Lower Kihansi plant. Even under the low-
growth forecast scenario, new short-terms generation will be necessary.
Qgagait B_alance
3.17 The capacity balance, (Figure 3-1 and Annex 3.3) shows that the margin between available
capacity and maximum demand was negative in 1992 and probably will remain negative in 1993 as well.
This is mainly due to the reduced hydro capacity as a result of the current drought conditions. Also, as
noted earlier, the delay in rehabilitating some of the existing diesel units has added to the problem and
forced TANESCO to shed peak loads. To alleviate the negative impact of load sheuding to the economy,
TANESCO is currently identifying options to reduce the demand through voluntary load shedding and
encouraging industnes with existing generatior. capacity to use their own generation. Furthernore, the
ESMAP demand management study and components in the proposed project to implement some of
ESMAP's recommendations will provide further assistance to TANESCO in managing peak loads and
energy consumption efficiently. The capacity balance also illustrates that capacity reserves will not
provide sufficient back-up until commissioning of the Lower Kihansi plant. Therefore, it will be
necessary to install additional thennal umts. The forecast indicates slightly positive capacity balance in
1994 and 1995, increasing in strength from 1996 onwards, assuming: (i) prompt rehabilitation of diesel
units (totalling 78 MW); (ii) installation of new thennal capacity in 1993 and shortly before the
commissioning of Lower Kihansi (up to 108 MW may be necessary); (iii) operation of all units at Kidatu
(totalling 204 MW) in 1996; (iv) full operation of Pangani redevelopment in 1996 (53 MW net addition
to capacity); and (v) commissioning of the Lower Kihansi scheme at the end of 1998. If, however, there
is a delay in some of the scheduled capacity additions, available capacity may fall below maximum
demand, making peak load shedding unavoidable. Moreover, if there is a delay in conmissioning Lower



-31 -
Kihans' by the end of 1998, TANESCO may need to install additonal themal generating capacity in the
late 1990s.

IV. THE ENVESTMENT PROGRAM AND THE PROJECT
The Investment Program
4.1             . The origin of the present investment program is the 1985 Power Sector
Development plan, updated and refined in 1990 by TANESCO's system planning consultants, Acres
Intemational, to take into account new information about the estimated supply and cost of natural gas
from the Songo Songo gas field. These least-cost analyses showed that the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric
project and a gas-fueled power plant were about equal in cost (Annex 4.1). Uncertainties about the
development of the Songo Songo gas field, however, made the Lower Kihmsi project more viable. The
Power Sector Development Plan also examined the option of power imports but concluded that, due to the
location of the load centers and the lack of substantial surplus generation capacity in either Kenya or
Uganda, power imports will not play a significant role in meeting future generation needs. The minor
exception is a plnned interconnection of the isolated diesel system at Bukoba with the nearby Ugandan
grid, which will improve the quality and reliability of supply to agro-industries and other energy
consumig activities in the Kagere region. Government officials and TANESCO discussed the contents of
the report with IDA and all parties have agreed that the Lower Kihansi Hydro Project would be the next
major investment in power generation.
4.2   In addition to long-term planning, IDA has helped TANESCO find a solution to the severe
shortage of generation that has led to frequent, widespread load-shedding which in turn has theatened to
stifle the economic recovery of the country. In response to a request from the Government, IDA sent a
mission in 1992 to study the problem. This mission recommended a set of short term measures to make
the best use of the water remaining in the hydro reservoirs, and the staged installation of several gas
turbines prior to the commissioning of the Lower Kihansi project. The effects of the generation shortage,
camsed by a drought, have been exacerbated by several coincidental technical problems. Planning studies
had predicted a potential shortfall of generation if a drought occurred, but in stuch a case estimated a
fairly small deficit. However, the drought that actually occurred coincided with: (i) higher load growth
than expected, due to the economic recovery; (ii) the extended outage of a unit at the Kidatu plant caused
by a generator fault; (iii) operational constraints caused by a defect in the design of the Mtera plant; and
(iv) delays, due to the Persian Gulf war, in the rehabilitation of diesel units. While recommending the
installation of new gas turbines, the mission recommended against rehabilitating the existing 13 MW gas
turbine, concluding that it has reached the end of its economic life.
4.3   Least-Cost Generation Plan. Tre revised least-cost generation development program for the
period 1992-1998 includes: (i) the rehabilitation of diesels connected to the grid (increasing capacity by
about 48 MW compared to 1991); (ii) the rehabilitation of the Kidatu hydroelectric power station
(increasing reliable capacity by 51 MW in 1996);A/ (iii) the redevelopment of the Pangani Falls
hydroelectric system (increasing capacity by 53 MW in 1996); (iv) construction of the first stage of the
Lower Kihani hydroelectric station for commissioning in 1998 (capacity 180 MW in 1998); and (v) the
8/    The station has four 51 Mw units installed. One had a damaged generator thai has been
repaired. The others will be taken out of service one after the other to upgrade excitation
systems in an effort to prevent a repeat of the failure.



- 34 -
installation of new gas turbines (36 MW in 1993, 18 MW in 1994, 18 MW in 1995 and 36 MW in
1997).2/
4.4   LeastCost Transmission Plan. The corresponding transmission investment program shows
construction of a second 220 kV line from Kidatu to Dar es Salaam to be the highest priority investment.
The line is needed to improve the reliability and voltage regulation of the supply to Dar es Salaam and to
accommodate rapid load growth in the capital city area. Construction of a 220 kV line from Singida to
Arusha is the second priority. The existing 132 kV line from Hale substation near Pangani falls to the
Moshi-Arusha region will be overloaded by 1993 or 1994. The line from Singida to Arusha will
strengthen the supply and have the additional advantage of creating a loop in the northeastern transmission
system, thus increasing reliability.
4.5   Least-Cost Distribution Plan. There is a need for substantial investments in the distribution
system to reduce network losses, improve reliability, and serve new loads. TANESCO has not given
sufficient attention to maintenance and expansion of the distribution network, mainly because of a lack of
finance. The least-cost development plan puts the focus first on the expansion, strengthening and
rehabilitation of networks in and around the four major load centers at Dar es Salaam, Arusha, Moshi and
Tanga, and then on the remaining networks, to reduce losses and improve the quality of service.
4.6   Investment Required. IDA and TANESCO defined and established the cost of the investment
program for the inerconnected system during the project's preappraisal.Q/ The appraisal mission
reviewed the investment program and agreed on its content with TANESCO. The Project includes all the
components for which TANESCO had not yet secured financing, except for the gas turbines required to
meet the present shortfall in capacity, rehabilitation of some existing diesels, and rehabilitation of
distribution networks outside the four main load ceters. The Government has recently secured financing
for two gas turbines totaling 36 MW and is discussing financing for another 18 MW unit with a donor.
4.7   TANESCO's proposed investment program for the isolated systems includes: (i) installing about
9 MW of gas turbines in the MtwaraALindi area to utilize gas from the Mnazi Bay natural gas discovery;
(ii) rehabilitating diesels; (iii) constrctng a 132 kV Transmission line between Bukoba and
Masaka(Uganda); (iv) developing a mini-hydro plant near Mto wa Mbu; and (v) various rural
electrification projects. The gas turbines and associated facilities at Mtwara/Lindi are included in the
project in a scheme that includes private sector participation. Financing has not yet been secured for the
other componens. TANE.SCO plans to seek financing to develop a simple planning tool and criteria to
examine the economic and financial viability of rural electrification schemes to help rank them for
potential financiers.
4.8 To develop both the interconnected and isolated systems according to least-cost economic
principles, TANESCO has agreed to provide justification to IDA and seek its concurrence before

Works in Progress
4.10 TANESCO has several projects underway in various parts of the country involving additions and
repairs to its facilities. The projects are: (i) phase II of a Diesel Operations and Maintenance Training
Project scheduled for completion in 1995; (ii) repair of exciters at Kidatu; (iii) reconstruction of the
Pangani Falls hydro station, expected to be completed in early 1996; (iv) rehabilitation of the remaining
two Pielstick Diesel generating units at the Ubungo station, scheduled for completion in 1993; (v)
construction of a second 220 KV transmission line from Kidatu to Morogoro scheduled for completion in
1993, and a 220 Kv line from Singida to Arusha on which construction has just started; and (vi) an
ongoing rural programs near Mbeya in the Southwest Zone.
Committed Projects
4.11   TANESCO has obtained funds to implement the following projects included in its 1992-1998
investment program: (i) construction of a mini-hydro scheme in an isolated area near Mto wa Mbu; (ii)
rehabilitation of the northwest grid in the vicinity of Dodoma; (iii) construction of the 220 KV line from
Morogoro to Dar es Salaam, a condnuation of the line from Kidatu which is under construction; (iv)
construction of a new 132 KV line from Bukoba in the Lake Zone to Masaka in Uganda, along with
distribution schemes in both the Lake and South West Zones; and (v) implementation of three rural
electrification schemes, one in the Lake Zone between Mwanza and Ngudu, another to supply six
ginneries in the Mwanza region from the grid, and the third to supply agricultural areas in the southern
part of the country.
-h he roect
4.12 Objectives. The main objectives of the proposed project correspond to Government policy
objectives for the power sector, which IDA supports. These are: (i) meeting the growing demand for
electricity at least-cost manner through the construction of the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric scheme; (ii)
helping the Government restructure the power sector to promote efficiency and private investment; (iii)
continuing the work already started on improving energy efficiency by reducing system losses, improving
service quality, and supporting demand side management initiatives; (iv) providing training and
technology to improve the skills and management effectiveness of staff in sector institutions; and (v)
promoting the development and operation of natural gas fueled generation by private investors.
4.13   Rationale for IDA Involvement. The proposed project addresses the 'rincipal issues in the power
sector. Increased generating capability and reliable service are essential for onomic development and
for the general well-being of the country. The Government has expressed its commitment to sector
reform and recognizes the need to increase TANESCO's autonomy for increased efficiency and
productivity. By remaining active in the power sector, IDA will be able to promote: (i) the expansion of



- 37 -
power supply based on least-cost criteria encompassing both supply and demand side options; (ii)
efficiency in energy supply and use; (iii) sound financial management and fiancial planning for the
sector through appropriate pricing and prudent capital structures; and (iv) capable national energy
planning and management through training studies and the importation of appropriate technologies.
Moreover, IDA has and will continue to play an important role in coordinating the traditonal sources of
financing while developing new sources in the private sector.
4.14   Project Preparadon. The Japan International Cooperation Agency (JICA) financed a detailed
feasibility study of the Lower Kihansi site. JICA's report, dated September 1990, forms the basis of the
Lower Kihansi development. An IDA mission visited Tanzania in October 1990 for pre-appraisal of the
project. During 1990, ESMAP helped TANESCO prepare the distribution component of the Project. A
draft report, first issued in December 1990 and reissued in November 1992, contains the results of this
work.
4.15 During pre-appraisal in January/February 1991, IDA and TANESCO analyzed the various
components of the Project in detail and agreed on the project's design. During that mission, the
Government and TANESCO requested IDA to finance, through a technical assistance credit, the detailed
design and preparation of bid documents for the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric scheme, as the
Government's request for financing from a different source was not being met. An IDA mission
appraised the Technical Assistance Project in May 1991 and IDA's Board approved it in January 1992.
A condition for appraising the proposed Power VI Project was the satisfactory completion by
TANESCO's consultants of a draft inception report, by June 1992, on the detailed design and latest cost
esdmates of the Lower Kihansi scheme to assure its economic viability. The consultants completed the
draft inception report in June 1992, and a Project Definition Report in Febmary 1993. The draft
inception report reconfinmed the cost estimates for Lower Kihansi and its attractiveness relative to the
competn Songo Songo gas-based project. Furthermore, TANESCO employed a Panel of Experts to get
an independent review of the design of the Lower Kihansi Project. The Panel reviewed the Project
Definition Report and visited the site in March 1993. Annex 4.11 shows the Panel's terms of reference,
and Annex 4.3 includes a summary of the panel's comments on the project design concept and
preparation. An appraisal mission visited Tanzania in June/July 1992 and, with cetain changes,
principally the deletion of rehabilitation of diesel engines, confirmed the project components agreed to
during preappraisal.
4.16 Project Description. The proposed project has three main parts: The first part, which
TANESCO wi1l implement, consists of:
*     the 180 MW first stage of the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric scheme;
*     expansion, reinforcement, and loss reduction in the distribution systems of major load
centers;



- 38 -
*     provision of vehicles, tools, and meters to improve distribution and commercial
operations;
*     completion of the rehabilitation of the Kidatu bydroelectric plant;
*     management support and training;
*     provision of computerized management infonrion systems and services to support
TANESCO's decentralized operations;
*     studies including tariff, assets valuaion, power system master plan, hydroelectric plant
feasibility, and distribution standards.
*     demand side management activities including audits and advertising;
*     upgrading of the dispatch center equipment and comuication systems; and
*     provision of workshop equipment.
The second part, which ZSFPC will implement, consists of:
*     a study of the operation of ZSPPC to define feasible options for its future structure;
-     provision of a commercial manager for ZSFPC to oversee commercial operations until the
utility is restuctured;
*     provision of prepayment meters ad associated materials to help improve collections.
The third part, which MWEM implement, consists of:
*     a study of the structure of the power sector and its associated regutory framework in
Tanzania to define options for restructug to improve efficiency, and to explore ways of
introducing private sector financing and management;
*     provision of consulting services to help promote private sector financing and operation of
thermal generation using gas from Songo Songo;
*     provision of eqpmt to help develop geneation from usmng the Mnmzi bay gasfield in a
joint arrangement with the private sector;
*     training and office technology for energy staff of the ministry.



-39-
A summary of these components follows. Annex 4.3 provides a more detailed description.
4.17 Lower Kiihansi Hydroelectric Scheme. The proposed scheme is located in southwestern Tanzania,
about 440 km from Dar es Salaamn. It utilizes the flow of the Kihansi River, which has its source on the
Central Plateau at about 2100 m above sea level. The river, in descending the Uzungwa Scarp, falls about
1000 m over five kllometers to the foot of the scarp, where it flows into the Kilombero River. The
proposed hydroelectric development consists of a low dam, at the head of the steepest section of the
scarp, diverdng flow through a power tumel, to an underground powerhouse, and then back into the
river at the foot of the escarpment. A head of 851 m is developed over a total conduit length of 5.4 km.
4.18  The concrete dam is designed for a maximum height of 25 m above foundation level, to provide a
live storage of some 870,000 m3 which is sufficient to provide for full daily regulation of flow for an
ultimate instlled capacity of 300 MW. This design provides for an intake adjacent to the dam on the left
bank to be connected to the headrace tunnel by a vertical shaft 240 m long and 25 mn in area. The
headrace tunnel would be 2900 m long and 25 i2 in area, with an inclination of 1 in 5.0, followed by a
125 m long steel lined setion leading to the powerhouse. The underground powerhouse cavem is
planmed to be 95.5 m long, 12.6 m wide and 30 m high, supported by systematic bolting and shotcreting.
From the powerhouse, individual outlet tunnels from each turbine would merge into a 1500 m long free
flow tun1el, which in turn would lead into an 800 m long canal, discharging back into the Kilansi River.
The powerhouse will ultmately accommodate five (initally three under the proposed project) Pelton
turbines, each developing 60 MW, with a power flow of 8.3 n/s, under a rated head of 845 m, with
vertical synchronous generators rated at 70.5 MVA at 0.85 power factor. The station would be connected
to the grid through two 220 kV transmission lines. One transmission line is phnned to connect with the
substation at Iringa, 113 km to the north, and the other one to the substation at Kidatu, 90 kn to the
northeast. These transmission lines also would serve to reinforce the transmission grid between Iringa
and Kidatu and so reduce the probability of total system collapse in the event of a fault in this part of the
grid.
4.19  Distribution Works. ESMAP, with the assistance of TANESCO staff, did extensive studies in
1991 to determine the needs of the distribution network. A report entitled, "Tanzania: Transmission and
Distribution System Technical Loss Reduction and Network Development," of March 1992, presents the
results of the stxdies which analyze the economic benefits of loss reduction and network expansion. The
proposed project includes the three main activities that the report recommends:
*     System Development and Reinforcement, focussing on reducing losses and increasing the
reliability of service to customers;
*     Rehabilitation and strengthening (including reducing losses) of existing facilities that have
deteriorated due to normal aging and, in some cases, insufficient maitenance; and
* System Expansion in the four main load centers to supply electricity to customers at
investment levels that yield a reasonable rate of return.



-40 -
Along with its primary goal of connecting new customers, the system expansion work will introduce new
technologies and demonstrate new standards and construction techniques resulting from studies described
later in this chapter.
4.20   Rehabilitation of the Kidatu Hydroelectric Station. The Kidatu generating station, which is 15
years old, requires major rehabilitation of the main generating equipment as well as the control and
auxiliary equipment, because the necessary foreign exchmge has not always been available to carry out
maintenance on time. Also, the generator of one of the units failed in October 1989. The apparent cause
of the failure was the design of the excitation system which resulted in carbon dust entering the windings.
A consulting firm studied the problem and recommended replacing all the exciters with static excitation
systems that had the additional advantage of providing better transient response. The firm also defined
other measures to catch up on deferred maitenance, modernize the control system and return the plant to
peak operating condition. After a delay in securing financing for the repair, SIDA, in 1991, agreed to
finance the urgent aspects of the work covering the repair of the faulty generator, replacement of all the
exciters and some work on the turbines. The defective generator was returned to service in late 1992.
The work on the remaining exciters and turbines will be done as soon as the units can be scheduled out of
service. The proposed project includes the less urgent work recommended, which SIDA financing did not
cover. It also includes the engineering services for supervising l hle work.
4.21   Exoet Services. TANESCO does not have sufficient ;taff to properly supervise the significant
volume of work planned during the period of the proposed p. oject, which will finance the several experts
required for this supervision. One expert will act as Project Manager, representing TANESCO's interests
in the construction of Lower Kihansi and the employment of this manager is a condition of effectiveness
of the proposed credit (para 7.3(iii)). In addition, TANESCO has retained a Panel of Experts to provide
guidance and advice on technical matters, safety and construction problems (para 4.15). The proposed
project also includes engineering services for preparing construction drawings and supervising the
construction of the Lower Kihansi hydro scheme. Expert services in Corporate Planning, Distribution
Plamning, Systems Improvement, Management and Personnel and Training, which the proposed project
also provides, will help strengthen TANESCO's institutional capability, implement its Productivity
Improvement Program, and rationalize its manpower resources.
4.22   Trainin,e. The project includes both fonnal and on-the-job training for TANESCO staff at all
levels, with particular emphasis on operation and  nhteance of generation, transmission and distribution
facilities, finance and accounting, planning, and management supervision. A new school, under
construction at Morogoro, will accommodate 70 students, for training in accounting, finance, commerce
and technical subjects. The project includes expansion of the facilities at Kidatu to provide additional
training for the operation and maintenance of the generating stations. Also, as further on-the-job training,
TANESCO will continue to assign staff to work with consultants and equipment ma er in offices
and             facilities overseas; this practice will continue on site during the project construction.
4.23    Manageenformation S        . TANESCO has started to upgrade its Accounrmg and
Customer Service systems using fiuds from the Power Engineering and Technical Assistance Credit.



- 41 -
Once completed, th.e new systems are expected to help reduce non-technical losses, improve collections,
and provide timely and accurate management information. The installation of the new systems correlates
with TANESCO's efforts to decentralize its operations. The proposed project aims to expand the
coverage of these systems and install necessary sub-systems that the Power Engineering Credit did not
include. The proposed project includes additional software, computers, meters, service material,
prepayment metering and vehicles. Also, to complement the accounting system, the proposed project
includes a new materials management system and a personnel management system.
4.24   Studies. The proposed project includes several financial and technical studies to help guide
development of the sector. The financial studies are a tariff study and an assets valuation study. The
former is needed especially to review the structure of tariffs and to determine how to restcture tariffs to
promote efficiency and conservation. The assets valuation study is needed since TANESCO has not done
a full engineering valuation for several years. Such a study is periodically necessary to correct structural
problems that accumulate when the asset value is updated using indices.
4.25 The main technical studies include one that will evaluate the potential of four hydroelectric sites
and bring the soundest two sites to feasibility level. Another study will consider these two sites along
with gas fired plants as well as other options, including any feasible, cost effective demand side
conservation options, in a power system master plan study. This study will include a comprehensive
revision of the load forecast model to reflect the significant changes in the economy that have occurred.
A third study, associated with significant training activities, will review TANESCO's present distribution
standards and explore ways of lowering the cost of connecting each customer without adversely affecting
the quality of electricity service supplied.
4.26 Demand Side Management (DSM). The project includes equipment, consulting services, training
and provisions for an information program to promote and support demand management. These inputs
will help implement work which ESMAP will define in detail. The overall approach to DSM is to focus
efforts on getting the maximum impact with a coordinated set of actions set in the context of Tanania's
present energy consumption patterns and institutional arrangements.
4.27 In a follow-up to its loss reduction study, ESMAP will begin work, in May 1993, to identify
specific DSM measures and formmlate an implementation strategy. The project will help carry out the
resulting strategy. The tariff increases that the Government has agreed to, as a condition for this project,
will provide the correct economic incentive for implementing cost effective DSM measures. One of the
outputs of the ESMAP work and the work in the proposed project will be a clear definition of the costs,
including adnistration and management, and potential energy saving of DSM measures for their explicit
consideration in the proposed power system master plan study.
4.28 Present indications are that the measures that will yield significant benefits will be in the industrial
and commercial sectors. At present, 541 large industial and commercial customers consume about 40
percent of total energy; about 35,000 small industrial and commercial customers consume 14 percent;
and 118,000 domestic customers consume 31 percent. Of the domestic customers, about 12,000 consume



- 42 -
50 percent of the energy for the group, that is 16 percent of the total. Therefore, the initial DSM efforts
will be focused on the large industrial and commercial customers. Annex 4.3 describes the proposed
DSM activities in greater detail.
4.29  DLspatch Center Upgrade. TANESCO has been unable to complete certain aspects of its Dispatch
Center because of limited finmcing. Therefore, the proposed project includes computer equipment to
give the dispatchers an off-line computing capability, and improvements to the communications systemi to
pemrit more information transfer into the center from remote stations. This will allow TANESCO to plan
the use of its energy resources better, especially in drought conditions.
4.30   Works_hps. The lack of proper workshop facilities has hindered repairs of mechanical and
electrical equipment. The proposed project will help to remedy this by providing adequate facilities in Dar
es Salaam, in the seven zones, at Mtera, Kidatu, and Pangani. Equipment for these facilities would
include a complete set of hand and power tools, drills, lathes, grinding machines, and welding equipment,
as well as safety equipment (gloves, hard hats, and goggles).
4.31 Project Components for Zanzibar. The proposed project will provide ZSFPC with a package
designed to start improving the commercial activities of the utility while a parallel study evaluates the best
option for its future management. Commercial improvements, along with tariffs that reflect real costs,
are essential to reduce the drain on the Island's treasury and enable ZSFPC to pay for the bulk supply of
electricity it receives from TANESCO. The package consists of providing, for about two years, a
commercial manager who would build on the commercial operations reform which ZSFPC has begun
with assistance from the African Development Bank. Also, the manager would supervise the installation
of prepayment meters, financed by the project, for almost half the customers on the island, and contribute
to the institutional study to be performed by consultants. Some of the options to be considered in the
study are privatizing the utility, if feasible, and performance based management contracting.
4.32   Assistance to MWEM. The project includes technical assistance for the Ministry of Water,
Energy and Minerals to improve the skills of and provide office technology for staff in its Department of
Energy and a study of the future structure of the power sector (para 2.39). Furthermore, two important
components designed to promote involvement of the private sector in natural gas fueled generation are
included.
4.33 The first component consists of preparation work, including consulting assistance, to help the
Ministry promote the development of the Songo Songo gas field by private investors. This component
will help evaluate project design alternatives and prepare, evaluate and negotiate contracts as necessary.
According to plans, the gas turbine which TANESCO will install will initially use diesel oil and then
convert to gas use after the development of the Songo Songo field. Since the capacity of the field is
sufficient to support generation of up to about 250 MW, the development should also contribute to fture
generation expansion needs and possibly to the export of power.
4.34   The second is to install a pilot gas fueled generation project at Mnazi Bay to serve the isolated
systems in the area and reduce the need for imported diesel fuel. This part of the project has several
important featues. First, it seeks to demonstrate a use for remote gas resources found in the course of
petroleum exploration for the domestic market; several other African countries have abandoned the
development of such resources. The plan is for the government to finance the necessary pipeline and



-43 -
power geneaon equipmt and as it to the private investor/operator, who would develop the gas weil
to Production and maage te gs and power generation failities. Remuneration for the investor would
ocr under a power sales contsa witb TANESCO, wich opeates the distibution system in the area.
Also, tet would be an dexn of the sales tariff, with its initial value being set though the invitation
of bids fbr the opration.

4.36 The estimated total cost of the proposed project, including physical and price contingencies but
excluding duties, taxes and interest during construction, is US$383.7 million, of which US$340.5 million
(89 percent) is foreign exchange and US$43.2 million (11 percent) is local cost. The esimated total
financing requireme, including interest during construction, is US$440.4 million. All project costs are
based on January 1993 prices, and most of the proposed project items include physical contingencies of
10 percent. The excepdons are dam and intake structures and powerhouse, 15 percent;
electromechanical equipment, 5 percent (based on recent quotations); and tunnels, 25 percent.
NORPLAN, the consulting firm designing the plan, based its estimate for the tunnels on a lrgely unlined
design. If geological conditions are not as good as expected, excavation costs and the proportion of the
tunnel to be lined will increase. The Panel of Experts, while agreeing with the amount of sub-surface
investigations carried out, noted in its first report that with the level of uncertainty of sub-surface
conditions, the estimates of cost and schedule are perhaps slightly optimistic.11/ Therefore, the
proposed project provides an increase in the level of contingencies for the tunneling works from the 15
percent recommended by the Consultants to a more conservative 25 percent. The price contingency
calculation is based on the total of base cost plus physicai contingencies, assuming domestic inflation rates
of 15 percent for 1993 and 10 percent for 1994 through 1996, and 10 percent thereafter. The
corresponding international inflaion rates are 3.9 percent for 1993 and 3.8 percent thereafter. The
assumed base exchange rate for January 1993 is 350 Tsh to the US Dollar.
4.37 NORPLAN prepared the project cost estimates for the Lower Kihansi scheme based on January
1991 prices. During the project appra  all costs were aligned to a common base of January 1993 by
adjusting for the exchange rate between the US$ and TSh, and international and local inflation rates.
TANESCO prepared the distribution component of the proposed project with the assistance of ESMAP,
operating on the assumption that foreign contractors would do the work on the substations, the 33 Kv
lines and about half of the distribution. The remaider would be carried out by TANESCO workers.
The cost estimate for rehabiiting the Kidatu hydroelectric station was prepared by consultants and
TANESCO and appears to be reasonable. The remaining components were prepared by TANESCO and
the appraisal mission based on quotations from suppliers and prices for similar goods and services
recently provided elsewhere.

4.39  The estimated total foreign exchange required for the proposed project is US$340.5 million of
which IDA will provide US$200 million (59 percent). The EIB has expressed interest in financing a part
of the electrical plant at Lower Kihansi. EIB's initial offer was up to about US$15 million, but IDA has
asked it to consider a complete procurement package. EIB plans to appraise the component in early May,
1993. ODA has agreed to finance the tariff study and assets valuation study for a total of US$ 3.1
million. If these amounts are taken to be the only funds available, there would be a financing gap of
US$122.4 million. Other donors, however, have expressed interest in financing various components,
although several will have to arrange it in the context of their bilateral negotiations with Tanzania.
NORAD has expressed interest in financing electromechanical components at Kihansi and is discussing
the matter with the Government now that it has identified energy as a priority area for assistance. The
Belgian Government has expressed interest in financing the transformers at Kihansi. KfW has started
discussions with the Government and at its request is considering financing the transmission lines and
substations to link the plant to the grid. The Government has approached DANIDA for financing to
cover feasibility studies. This should be possible by reallocating funds allocated to existing slow-moving
programs. TANESCO expects private company participation of about US$3.0 million, through a
competitive bidding process, in financing the Mnazi Bay gas development. Taking these into account, the
financing gap becomes about US$5.8 million, a more manageable figure given that some Donors, for
example ODA, have indicated that they may be able to include components that disburse later in the
project schedule in subsequent rounds of cofinancing arraigements. Although all the components of the
proposed project are of high priority, if there is a shortfall in the tentative cofnancing scheme outlined in
table 4.3, IDA is prepared to reallocate its resources, principally from distribution and generation
rehabilitation components, to assure completion of the Kihansi project.
4.40   To complete the financing arrangements, the Government has agreed to continue to approach
donors and place a high priority on financing for the project componeniz. Satisfactory progress in making
cofinancing arrangements and the execution of an onlending agreement with TANESCO are conditions of
the Credit's effectiveness (para 7.3(ii)). The onlending terms for TANESCO are an amount of US$180
million at an interest rate of aot less than 7.43 percent with repayment in 20 years, including five years of
grace. TANESCO will bear the exchange risk. The Government will onlend US$2 million to ZSFPC
umder the same terms. ZSFPC's disbursement of the funds is contingent on the execution of a satisfactory
onlending agreement, and the Government of Zanzibar maintaining the average electricity tariff in line
with TANESCO's average retail tariff (para 7.4).
Components for Implementation by TANESCO
4.41   TANESCO will implement most of the power components of the proposed project. Annex 4.7
provides a Project ImplementatioL Schedule. TANESCO has agreed to establish a project management
unit (PMU) and appoint a Project Manager from its existing staff for the proposed project, who would
have the following responsibilities: (i) coordinating the supervision of the proposed project's execution;
(ii) liaising with IDA and the co-financiers on the various components of the proposed project; (iii)
dealing with all correspondence, procurement activities and progress reports; and (iv) liaising with
consultants to be employed for the supervision of Kihansi and the distribution work. Appointment of a
Project Manager, with qualifications acceptable to IDA, and establishment of the project management unit
are conditions of the Credit's effectiveness (para 7.3(iv)).



- 48 -
4.42 Lower Kihansi Hydroelectric Plant. TANESCO hlred consultants in January 1992 (financed by
Credit 2330-TA) to do field surveys and subsurface explorations, as well as prepare detailed designs and
cotact documents for Lower Kihansi. According to the present schedule, TANESCO will award the
consultancy contract for construction stage services by mid 1993. There will be two civil contracts: one
for construction of access roads and construction camp infrastucture, and the second for the main civil
works. The award of the preliminary works contract should take place in the third quarter of 1993. To
meet this schedule, TANESCO has adopted post-qualification of contractors, based on clearly defined and
stringent criteria. Designs and documents for the main civil contact will take longer to prepare, since
they are dependant on the site investigations which TANESCO completed in October 1992. Bidders for
this contract, which is expected to be awarded in March 1994, will be prequalified. While consultants
will be responsible for fulfilling the role of the engineer in construction supervision, TANESCO, with
funds from the proposed project, will hire an experienced, externally recruited, project manager to assist
the utility in managing the activities at Kihansi. Employment of the project manager is a condition of the
Credit's effectiveness (para 7.3 (iii)). In order to assure the continued safety of the proposed dam and
strucures, TANESCO agreed to send to IDA, no later than one year before the expected completion of
the structures, details of its arrangements for inspecting and maintaning the dam and associated structues
(Schedule 1).
4.43 Construction of the project should take a total of 58 months from the award of the preliminary
works contract. The critical path passes through the access and camp infrastructure facilities, access
tunel to the powerhouse, powerhouse excavation and equipment installation.
4.44   Rehabilitaton of Kidatu. Contractors under the supervision of a project management consultant
employed by TANESCO will do the rehabilitation work on the Kidatu hydroelectric plant.
4.45  Improvement of the Distribution Network. The present schedWe calls for consultants to design
and prepare bid documents for the rehabilitation and construction of the distribution network early in
1994. TANESCO proposes to retain the services of a consulting firm to organize and manage the
proposed distribution rehabilitation work. In addition, the firm will have to review TANESCO's present
way of organizing and performing distribution work, and help introduce new techniques and tools to
improve productivity. Terms of reference for the consulting services are given in Annex 8.1.
4.46 TANESCO and contractors are expected to start construction early in 1995 and zomplete it at a
uniform pace by the end of 1997. On-the-job training of TANESCO staff is expected to reach a stage
whereby they would be able to carry on with the distribution work after the consultants have left.
4.47 Distribution PlanninS. A consultant will assist the distribution planning group formed by ESMAP
and expand the activities of this group to the Zonal offices. The intention is for the headquarters group to
be responsible for setting distribution standards based on economic criteria, and for the zonal offices to
apply these standards and maintain t;   ibases in their Zones. Terms of reference for the consultant
are in Annex 8.2. These training actlvtlies are scheduled for the period 1993 to 1994 and should bring
TANESCO staff to a level to enable them to continue the planning work after the consultant has left.

training at Eastern and Southern Africa Management Insitute (ESAMI) that took place in the latter part of

staff and line constuction workers may begin. An expatriate instructor for each group would prepare a

Project Preparation Facility (PPP)
4.59 IDA has approved a PPF of US$ 750,000 equivalent to assist the Government finance part of the
detailed design for the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric scheme. In the event that the proposed credit does
not become effective, the Government would have to reimburse IDA in accordance with the standard
clauses in the PPF Agreement.
Disbursements
4.60  The disbursement of the proposed credit proceeds should take place over a period of 66 months,
from July 1993 through December 1998. Disbursements from the proposed credit will be in accordance
with the disbursement procedures of IDA and will be made for 100 percent of foreign expenditures for
those components which IDA will finance. There will be no disbursement of proceeds from the proposed
credit against local expenditures. For contracts of less than US$200,000, IDA will allow simplified
disbursement procedures on the basis of Statements of Expenditure. On the basis of estimated anmual
project costs, Annex 4.9 gives anticipated disbursements, which match closely the standard profiles for
Bank loans and IDA credits for power projects in the Africa region. The closing date for the Credit is
June 30, 2000.
Special Account
4.61  In order to ensure the timely availability of funds, TANESCO will open a Special Account in a
banldng institution acceptable to IDA, in the amount of US$ 1,500,000; this amount is equal to an
estimted four months of average project expenditures, excluding direct disbursements under major
contracts. Funds in the Special Account will be available to finance only eligible expenditures under the
project. IDA will replenish the Special Account as needed.
Environmental Asects
4.62   The Lower Kihansi hydroelectric scheme is a Category A project for Environmental Assessment
(EA). Consultants, on behalf of the government and TANESCO, prepared the EA in stages between
1989 and 1992. Concerned departments of the Government and TANESCO were involved, and extensive
discussions with representatives of vaious interest groups have taken place in Tanzania. The final EA
report, dated February 28, 1992, is available for review at TANESCO's headquarters in Dar es Salaam.
Annex 4.10 gives a summary of this report. Most importantly, the Lower Kihansi dam will inundate 30
hectares and create a pondage of 1.4 million cubic meters. Roughly 50 percent of the affected area is
agricultnral land, and the remaining 50 percent is second-growth natural forest. The project will require
the removal of two dwellings, which are occupied temporarily. The run-of-river project is not expected
to have a serious downstream impact on wildlife or fisheries. The expected effects on flora and fauna
should be minimal and temporary. The scheme will not adversely affect water quality, although an
indirect effect of improved access to water may be intensified agriculture, which may have some adverse
consequences over the long run. The project will not increase water-related diseases. However, there is
a distinct hreat that members of the labor force may introduce AIDS into the remote and sparsely
populated region. To minimize that risk, bid documents for civil works would require that the bidders
propose effective measures to control the spreading of AIDS during the construction perod. Safe
diposal of material excavated for the intakes, penstock and ta,trace tunnel may pose an environmental



- 53 -
problem of limited magnitude. However, TANESCO has agreed to implement, by June 30, 1994, an
Environmena Mitigation Plan for the Lower Kihansi Scheme, and the transmission line from Lower
Kihansi to Iringa and from Lower Kihansi to Kidatu (Schedule 1). Furthermore, TANESCO, with the
help of consultas, has completed a separate Enviromnental Assessment of the trarsmission lines between
the Lower Kihansi power plant and Iringa and Kidatu (Schedule 1).
Project Risks
4.63 The inclusion of substantial project management inputs by specialist consultants for the critical
components should help minimize various risks to the proposed project's implementation. Nevertheless,
because of Tanzania's overall economic situation and the nature of the work, the following present higher
than usual risks:
*     financing for critical project components;
*     delays in commissioning of the Lower Kihansi hydroelectric plant due to unforeseen
geological conditions;
*     difficulties in completing institutional reforms.
4.64 inancing. The project requires substantial foreign currency financing which the Govermnent and
TANESCO expect to come from at least six multilateral and bilateral institutions other than IDA. But it
may be difficult to coordinate this arrangement, as priorities of the institutions sometimes change. In
order to minimize the risk of disruption to the major project component, the Lower Kihansi plant, IDA is
prepared to reallocate resources from less critical components. These resources would come principally
ftom distribution and generation rehabilitation components and if necessary from some of the institutional
strengthening components. The Government is conducting intensive discussions with the donors involved
and they have indicated a willingness to provide support. Satisfactory progress by the Govermment in
finalizing co-financing arrangements is a condition of effectiveness of the project.
4.65 Commissioning Delays. Because of the difficult terrain at the Lower Kihansi site, the consultants
have done sufficient investigations to pemt design, costing, and tendering, viewing the construction
process as the final definitive exploration and definition of subsurface conditions. The Independent Panel
of Experts endorsed this approach as appropriate for the conditions and noted that it requires a flexible
risk-sharing contractual philosophy and strict supervision. The Panel will review the contract documents
to assure adoption of the correct contractual approach and will review progress of the project regularly.
These arrangements, coupled with the project management resources that the project provides, should
permit prompt adjustments in response to unforeseen problems. If the problems are serious enough to
affect the critical path of the project and a delay is inevitable, the scheduling is such that TANESCO
should be able to respond by increasing the capacity of the short-delivery gas turbines it plans to install in
the period prior to the commissioning of Kihansi. This would limit the potentially deletrious impact of
unserved demand.
4.66 Difficulties With Institutional Reforms. The project envisages significant institutional
restun, which should result from the study on the sture of the power sector that the
Government has plamed. These reforms are usually difficult to implement because they affect control of



-54 -
the sector and often have significant impacts on employment and personnel practices. However, it is
possible to reduce the risk of failure by maiaWning a clear view of the    objectves and
carefly crafing the transition to minize any harmfWl hpact on the stakeholders in the process.
Moreover, the Governm    will mneimize the risks by employing competent consultants to plan the
tsition to the agreed stucture. The other major risk is the perception of country risks by potential
investors since the participation of the private sector will be an integal part of sector reform. The
carrying out of the Govermment's overall macrconomic policy reform program, and its planned
adopton of a well-defined reglatory framework along with IDA's conminng dialog with the Government
should help reduce these risks.
Proect Monitoring and S rvsion
4.67   During project preparadon, the Government, IDA and TANESCO discussed and agreed on the
records and reports necessary to monitor the progress of the project and the achievements of its
objectives. During the lifedme of the project, there will be a mnimum of two supervision missions per
year to monitor target dates for project implementation as well as costs and compliance with legal
covenants. There would also be close attention paid to peformance indicators and monitoring guidelines,
which Annexes 2.3 and 4.8 provide. The Govenment and IDA have agreed that as part of normal
supervision of the proposed project, there would be a midterm review in mid-1996, with the participation
of the Government, IDA, TANESCO and other co;nmfciers (para 7. 1(vi)). The main purpose of the
review will be twofold: (i) to conduct a comprehensive asessment of progress of physical compones
and agree on actions required, if any, to put the implementation on schedule; and (ii) to ensure that
TANESCO has taken or has received permission to take the necessary actions to meet their obligations
under the project, particarly in the areas of corporate aunomy, tauiffs, compeitive remuneration
paages for its employees, and improved collections. Some of TANESCO staff would benefit from
brefins on Bank's procurement pwoceue at hedquarts prior to project launching. Futher,
discussion of the reporting requirems will take place durng project lamnchig soon after the declaraton
of effectiveness of the project. TANESCO will prepare a Project Compltion Report within six months
of project completion.
hfrfn Review.
4.68 Two major objectives of the proposed project are to improve the performance of TANESCO and
achieve its partidal privatization. Thus, implemation of the instional component (Chapter 2) is
etremely impcrtant. The Government and TANESCO concur with this assessment, and the Government
has agreed with IDA that no later than June 30, 1994, there would be a joint review of the proposed
project by the Govemment, TANESCO, IDA and the co-financiers to assess the progress achieved in
meeting the key conditions designed to improve TANESCO's perfomance, including partial divestiture of
its operations (pam 7. 1(vii)). The purpose of the review, jW ALa, would be to assess whether
TANESCO has: (I) inaeased and maintied the average tariff stipulated in the legal documents; (ii)
reduced its accounts receivable to about 75 days of sales revenue; (iii) implemented the agreed staffing
proposals (para 2.17); and (iv) offered its consuction business for sale. There wil be a discussion of
the findings and reom  ions of the consultants ened by the Goverment to study the stunre of
the power sector, including possible divestitre of TANESCO's distibuton tems, at this time and IDA
will agmee with the Government on the imple tion of the findings. The Gevrment has agreed that
following this agreemen it will promptly implement the conultants'  ons (pan 2.39). The
timing of the interim review is such that only about half of IDA's finds will have been committed at this
point.



V. FINANCIAL ASPECTS AND COST RECOVERY
Overview
5.1   Since 1988, the Government of Tanzania and TANESCO have made a steady effort to improve
TANESCO's financial position and operating results following two crises which have had an adverse
impact on TANESCO's finances. The first crisis was a major devaluation of the Tanzanian Shilling,
beginning in 1986, and the deleterious impact it had on the servicing of TANESCO's debt in foreign
currency. The second crisis was a drought which substantially increased the average cost of electricity
generation because of the need to use high-cost thermal generation to make up for unavailable lower-cost
hydropower.
5.2   Following a major devaluation of the TSh, which began in 1986, the Government and
TANESCO, in 1988, embarked on a restructuring program which consisted of: (i) rescheduling
govermnent loans to TANESCO; (ii) an immediate tariff increase of 45 percent followed by regular tariff
increases of 15 percent semi-annually (in local currency terms); and (iii) conversion into equity of grants
which the Government had received from foreign donors and had onlent to TANESCO. These actions
helped to improve TANESCO's financial results over the 1989-1991 period.
5.3    After improvements in TANESCO's financial results during 1989-91, a drought, which began in
1991, increased reliance on costly diesel generation and load shedding, at times equivalent to nearly one
third of available hydro generating capacity (para 4.2). Consequently, the regular tariff increases which
the Government had adopted as part of TANESCO's financial restructuring bave not been sufficient to
offset higher costs resulting from drought conditions. In response to this second crisis, the Government
recenly increased the average electricity tariff from the equivalent of about US$ 0.06 to US$ 0.07 per
kWh. Furthermore, to ensure continued improvement in TANESCO's financial results, TANESCO,
under the proposed project has agreed to: (i) increase the average tariff to U$ 0.09 as a condition of
effectiveness of the project; (ii) establish annual tariff increases in US$ terms indexed to the international
iflation rate; (iii) implement the results of studies on appropriate tariffs and asset revaluation; and (iv)
include, within the proposed project, components to complete the work started on improving TANESCO's
billing, collection and accounting activities.
Financial Position and Past Operating Results
5.4   The operating results and financial position of TANESCO for the 1988-1990 period are based on
audited accounts. The results for 1991 are based on actual and estimated data. Table S.1 summarizes
TANESCO's financial performance during the 1988-1991 period.

5.5   TANESCO has experienced a tight cash siuation in recent years due to (i) devaluation of the Tsh
relative to the US$, making it difficult for TANESCO to service its external debt; (ii) high account
receivable; and (ii) increases in operatn costs following severe drought conditions which began in 1991,
requiring increased use of thermal generation capacity. Initially, after the 1986 devaluation, TANESCO
was able to make payments on the foreign currency loans it had contracted directly but deferred some of
the payments due on grants which the Government had received but had relent to TANESCO as loans.
Also, after meeting operating expenses, TANESCO was able to finance most of the local cost of its
capital expenditures. But by 1988, TANESCO realized the need to take action to mitigate the impact of
the devaluation on its debt servicing capability and establish a more solid financial base.
5.6   In 1988, the Government and TANESCO embarked on a financial restrucuring program. First,
the Government rescheduled some of its loans to TANESCO and converted into equity some of
TANESCO's overdue debt to the Government. Second, the Goveroment allowed TANESCO to increase
the average electricity tariff in domestic currency terms by 45% in mid-1988 and establish semi-annual
tariff increases thereafter. Third, the Government converted into equity some of the grants it originally
had received from foreign donors but had relent to TANESCO. Improvements to TANESCO's fiancial
situation enabling TANESCO to service its debt entirely, resulted from implementing this package of
financial measures combined with automatic, semi-annual tariff increases of 15% during 1989 to 1991.
5.7 Beginning in 1992, the increased use of thermal generation began to increase TANESCO's
operating costs, but TANESCO recently raised its average tariff from US$ 0.06 to US$ 0.07 equivalent to
help offset these higher costs. Purthermore, as a condition of effectiveness of the proposed project,
TANESCO has agreed to another tariff increase, to US$ 0.09 per kWh which is expected by the end of
July 1993, and to apply an annual rate of increase equal to projected US$ inflation until it implements
the results of a proposed tariff stdy.

5.8   The conversion of some Govermment loans into equity has increased TANESCO's equity base
which requies cautious interpretation, keeping in mind that TANESCO, in recent years, has not had a
professional revaluation of its assets, which would include, inter al. a detailed assessment of all
equipment in operation. Pending such an evaluation, TANESCO has applied a yearly index to reflect
domestic and international inflation as well as devaluation. This may overstate the company's equity
since the annual revaluation by index does not separate out old assets which are no longer in service. An
overstatement of the company's equity implies a better company performance in reducing its debt:equity
ratio and an understatement of performance in the net return it received on its fixed assets. Nevertheless,
taking the data in Table 5.2 as the best approximation at the present time, TANESCO's debt:equity ratio
improved from 53:47 in 1988 to 40:60 in 1991 and the rate of return on average net fixed assets
increased from % in 1988 to 6.8 % in 1991. In order to construct a firmer base for measuring
TANESCO's financial performance, ODA has agreed to finance a detailed asset valuation study along
with a tariff study. TANESCO has agreed to: (i) engage consultants by June 30, 1994, to perform the
Asset Revaluation Study under terms of reference satisfactory to IDA; (ii) funish the draft
recommendations of the study to IDA for its review and comments; (iii) finalize the study taking IDA's
comments into account; (iv) implement the recommendaDons of the study within six months of the
study's completion; and (v) annually, thereafter, revalue its assets according to the methodology
recommended in the study (Schedule 1).



58-
Accuni  and Audit
5.9 The present accounting procedures in TANESCO involve too many manual transactions and
insufficient intnal control, resulting in difficulties in reconciling the accounts and closing the books on
time. The Power Rehabilitation Project made an attempt to improve the accounting function by providing
two financial experts (Computer Advisor and Financial Advisor) to assist TANESCO in strengthening its
Finance Directorate, but this attempt failed. The reasons for this failure were that the Computer Advisor
TANESCO hired left after only five months of service, and TANESCO terminated the Financial
Advisor's contract after one year due to poor performance. TANESCO has experienced difficulty closing
its books on time due to frequent breakdowns of the old mainframe computer, a lack of cmpetent
computer personnel and an out-of-date accounting system. is a result, completion of the audit for 1988
accounts did not take place until about a year after the end of the financial year, and the audit for 1989
accounts was not complete until October 1991. TANESCO submitted its 1990 audited accounts to IDA
only in February 1993 and now expects that 1991 audited accounts will be available by June 1993. To
achieve the requisite improvements in the prparation of its accounts, TANESCO, with the assistance of
consultants, will be installing a modern computerized accounting system (financed under the recently-
approved Power Engineering and Technical Assistance Credit). The new computerized accounting
procedures should be in place during the 1994 financial year.
5.10   The external auditors for TANESCO, as well as for other parastatals in Tanzania, are the
Government-owned Tanzania Audit Corporation (TAC). Tbe present auditing arrangement is satisfactory
to IDA. IDA and TANESCO have agreed that qualified auditors will audit TANESCO's accounts,
including the Project Account, Special Account and the Stateme  of Expenditures, and tha TANESCO
would send the reports to IDA, within ten months after the 1993 financial year and within six months
after the end of the financial year for every financial year thereafter.
Non-Technical Losses and Billing
5.11 A 1992 ESMAP study estimated TANESCO's non-technical losses at 10 percent of net
generation. These losses are due mainly to illegal connections, meter tampering, inefficient meter
reading, improper reporting of customers in the billing system, and various problems with TANESCO's
computerized billing system, including keypunch errors and tampering with the computer progmms.
These deficiencies in TANESCO's commercial operations have caused losses which have reduced
TANESCO's revenue. Moreover, inefficiencies in the outdated, mainframe-based billing system, and
difficulties in counicating with customers in various parts of the country have led to poor customer
service.
5.12 TANESCO recognized the above problems in 1989 and dismissed several employees suspected of
tampering with the billing system. As a part of its Productivity Improvement Program (PIP), TANESCO
assigned more capable, better-paid staff to read meters and changed meter reading routes frequently to
avoid collusion with customers. Also, TANESCO strengthened its inspection crews and assigned senior
nunagers to monitor the meter reading process weekly. Pollowing the 1992 ESMAP i+udy, TANESCO
established crews to check the integrity of customer databases and to check for illegal connections.
Despite these efforts, TANESCO's inadequate computer systems and software hindered the company from
making significant and ltng progress in controlling non-technical losses and providing proper customer
service.
5.13  IDA, through its Power Engineering and Technical Assistance Credit in 1992, provided financing
for conslting services to help TANESCO select and install modern billing software and computers to



- 59 -
manage its customer services. TANESCO has selected consultants and the work should start in mid
1993. There will be a decentralization of the billing system to support the decentralized organizational
structure now in place, which makes the manager in each of the seven distribution zones fully accountable
for the individual zone's operating results. The billing system software will allow the use of
microcomputers to facilitate eventual decentralization to the level of small local offices which will have
customer records in an on-line environment to facilitate prompt response to customer enquiries.
Furthermore, the software will have several important security and audit functions to prevent software
tampering and to facilitate monitoring customer usage. The various billing centers will operate
independently at first, with periodic consolidation of records, and with subsequent interconnection within
a network once communications improve. The consultants' terms of reference require them to help
TANESCO re-establish the integrity of the customer database as they install the new system and, in the
process, reduce non-technical losses immediately. The proposed project tncludes meters, service
materials, vehicles and tools to aid in this effort along with additional software and hardware to expand
the coverage of the system.
Collections
5.14 The second major problem TANESCO faces in increasing its revenue is difficulties with
collecing revenues once it issues bills. The latest information available relating receivables to total
billing is for 1991. Total billing in that year was about TSh 15,409 millior. compared to total receivables
in December 1991 of about TSh 9,073 million, and these are equivalent to about 215 days of billing.
More recent figures are available for receivables only, and Table 5.3 provides a breakdown of these by
customer as of March 1992. Some of the arrears (about 10%) have been outstanding for over two years
and TANESCO will probably have to write them off. To improve collections, TANESCO, in recent
years, has started to disconnect most categories of delinquent customers. The campaign was successful
initially, but the continuing inability of the computer system to produce accurate, timely bills hampered
progress. Also, TANESCO was forced to dismiss the head of its computer unit for alleged dishonesty
and was unable to find a suitable replacement immediately. The new billing system will help solve the
collection problem sine it provides the custoner service function at several centers in the zones and no
single staff member will be able to subvert the system. Also, the Consultants' terms of reference require
the installation of a pilot pre-payment metering system. This system has the advantage of requiring the
customer to pay before using the service and therefore the utility does not carry receivables.
