5.15 Table 5.3 shows that Parastatals owed the most to TANESCO, accounting for 31 percent of
accoumts receivable as of March 1992. The shares of other customers in total accounts receivable were:
30 percent for private customers, 13 percent for the Water supply company, and 12 percent for the
Zanzibar State Fuel and Power Corporat5on (ZSFPC). At appraisal, the outstanding bills of Government
agencies and Parastatals amounted to TSh 2.2 billion and TSh 3.3 billion respectively. The Government
settled Tsh 1.1 billion of the outstanding bills of Government agencies prior to negotiations. TANESCO
has had difficulty in having the remaining outstanding bills certified for payment because it involves
sending staff into most major towns in the country. So far, the Government has paid Tsh 263 million of
the 1.1 billion and has issued promissory notes, in the amount of the outstanding bills, that TANESCO
can convert to cash by presenting them along with the certified bills to the Treasury. To ensure that the
matter is fully resolved, TANESCO's completion of the certification of the bills and the government's
payment of all the certified bills is a condition of effectiveness of the project (para 2.3). The Government
has settled the TSh 2.2 billion owed by Government agencies and has agreed to settle the arrears of the
Parastatals by monthly payments from April 30, 1992 to April 30, 1993 in equal installments.
5.16  In the past, bec;use of limited information processing facilities, TANESCO concentrated its
efforts on large customers. It also targeted various zones to clear up the accounts for smaller customers
and rewarded staff performance through the Productivity Improvement Program. The new billing system
will enhance these efforts by making it much easier to track delinquent customers. Also, with the
installation of a new accounting system (para. 5.9) TANESCO will be able to write off any unrecoverable
accounts.
5.17   The Government has agreed to allow TANESCO to collect aggressively from Government
agencies. Nevertheless, there are some agencies that TANESCO cannot disconnect for humanitaian
reasons. So the Government and TANESCO have agreed with IDA that: (i) starting September 30,
1993, the Government will pay TANESCO directly, every quarter, the electricity bills of those customers
that TANESCO cannot disconnect for humanitarian reasons; (ii) TANESCO diligently will enforce the
plan of action for collecting overdue accounts from govermnent agencies and Parastatals and its policy of
disconnecting delinquent private customers; (iii) TANESCO wfll prepare a quarterly report summarizg
its outstanding customer accounts for IDA's review; and (iv) TANESCO will gradually reduce its
accounts receivable from the present level to no more than 60 days of sales by December 31, 1994 and
maintain it at that level thereafter (Schedule 1).
5.18 The Zanzibar State Fuel and Power Company (ZSFPC), since 1985, has been receiving bulk
power from TANESCO to supply its consumers. Until 1991, an interim tariff was in effect. The basis
of this tariff, outlined in the contract between TANESCO and ZSFPC, was a supply cost accounting for
the fact that ZSFPC had paid the capital cost of the submarine cable and equipment associated with the
interconnection. TANESCO and ZSFPC agreed to revise the tariff on January 22, 1991. TANESCO
adjusted the average tariff to ZSFPC at a base price of TSh 3.9 per kWh for 1990, subject to firtuer
annual adjustment using a fornmla based on the economic cost of generation and transmission in the grid.
Using this formula, the two utilities agreed that ZSFPC owed TANESCO TSh 1.4 billion, including the
back payments. Therefore, the Government has agreed: (i) to ensure that ZSFPC settles its overdue
accounts with TANESCO; (ii) to compensate TANESCO for any shortfall in payments in future; and (iii)
to cause ZSFPC to comply with its tariff agreement with TANESCO (para 7. 1(ix)). ZSFPC has been
short of cash and unable to pay the overdue accounts in filll. But it al-eady has made a payment of TSh
400 million and has promised to pay the remainder in equal monthly installments over two years. Also,
IDA has agreed to help ZSFPC improve its management to improve its capacity to earn reasonable
revenues. The assistance includes a commercial manager to start improving the customer service system



- 61 -
while he manages a study by consultants of appropriate future management arrangements (including
cotact management), and the installation of prepayment imetering for about half of the customer
population. Disbursement of the funds for these activities is contingent on ZSFPC raising its retail tariff
to the level of that prevailing on the mainland (para 7.4).
Tariffs. Structure and Levels
5.19   The demand and sales of electricity have increased substantially, due in part to the success of the
implementation of the Economic Recovery Program, but also because of deficiencies in the existing tariff
structure. A tariff study which Cr. 1405-TA financed, produced recommendations which the Government
implemented in 1986. Since then, the Government has increased average tariffs regularly but the
structure has gradually become distorted. The present tariff structure favors domestic consumers, and is
discriminatory with respect to commercial users, with rates for the latter more thar f-'ur times higher than
domestic ones. This apparently has led to widespread misuse and faulty end-use ciassifications.
Moreover, because uniform tariffs are applied throughout vanous regions of Tanzania, a substantial cross-
subsidy exists between consumers which the interconnected system supplies and those who receive
supplie. from isolated diesel plants where costs are much higher. However, it is estimated that the
isolated systems account for less thar. 5 percent of total electricity sales.
5.20  In January 1992, the Government made some adjustments to the tariff structure by differentiating
the unit energy charges at each consumption level, depending on the total monthly consumption, and
increasing the charges for the initial consumption for larger consumers within a tariff category. Annex
5.1 outlines the tariff level and structure effective from January 1992. This structure provided for an
average charge of about TSh 28 per kWh (US$.09) for high voltage consumers and an average charge of
TSh 7.2 per kWh (US$.02) for residential consumers. The structure also subsidizes smaller commercial
and industrial consumers, as well as agriculture, public lighting and supplies to the water authority
(NUWA). Furthermore, the unit energy rates for residental consumption of up to 1,000 kWh per month
were very low, between US$.01 and US$.03 depending on the level of total monthly consumption.
Although low tariffs, offering poor households the benefit of electricity, may be justified on social
grounds, there is no apparent justification for subsidizing consumers that use more than 100 kWh per
month. Also, TANESCO subsidizes light industrial and commercial consumers which consume up to
1,000 kWh per month. This is not warranted because such enterprises have the ability to pay for their
consumpton. TANESCO, however, has introduced measures designed to conserve energy such as: (i)
increased incremental unit energy price to residential, small commercial and light industrial consumers
with higher consumption.
5.21   Despite the above adjustments in the structure of TANESCO's tariff, it needs careful review and
streamlining. Moreover, a fuel-cost adjustment mechanism could be useful to take into account the
variation in fuel cost resulting from changes in hydrology. To address all the tariff related issues,
TANESCO has agreed to engage consultants to recommend an appropriate tariff structure and level.
ODA has agreed to finance the study. TANESCO has agreed to take into account IDA's views in
finalizing the terms of reference as well as the recommendations of the study. TANESCO also agreed to
conplete the study by June 30, 1994, with the implementation of the agreed recommendations to occur
within six months of the study's completion (Schedule 1). TANESCO also agreed to update the tariff
regularly based on the methodology proposed by the study.
5.22 IDA has made a preliminary estimate of the Long Run Average Incremental Cost (LRAIC) as a
proxy for the LRMC of the system, based on the present least-cost investment program. The calculation



-62 -
of the LRAIC represents the average incremental economic cost of meeting increases in demand,
estimated to be between US$ 0.09 and US$ 0.10 equivalent per kWh sold, expressed in end-1992 prices
and at a discount rate of 12 percent.
Future Financial Operations and Position
5.23 Annexes 5.2 to 5.5 show projected financial statements of TANESCO for 1991 through 1998,
together with notes and assumptions used in these statements, while Table 5.4 provides the major
financial performance indicators for 1992-98. The basis of these projections is a set of assumptions
conceming the revalued asset base, domestic inflation, currency exchange rates and load growth forecast,
which are all subject to some uncertainty (Annex 5.5). Under the Base Case load growth scenario,
resulting from the load forecast which consultants (Acres International Ltd. of Canada) prepared, energy
supply will grow approximately 40 percent over the project period (1993-1998). Projected balance sheets
for the years ending Decmber 31, 1992 through 1998 show that, under the above assumptions, the
debt/equity ratio will increase gradually from 46:54 in 1992 to 53:47 in 1997, reflecting the need for
increased borrowing to finance the large investments required. But in 1998, the debt/equity ratio will
drop to 52:48 following the completion of major investments and repayment of loans. IDA considers
TANESCO's debt/equity ratio satisfactory for the period under review.

5.24 TANESCO's average tariff in 1992 was about the equivalent of US$ 0.06 per kWh. Projections
of future tariff levels are based on two criteria. First, TANESCO must have a tariff that allows it to eam
sufficient revenue to cover its operations cost, its debt service and contribute a reasonable amount to
future investment. Second, the tariff level should be close to the LRMC to provide the correct economic
signals to users for efficient electricity use. FinancW projections show that TANESCO needs to increase
its average tariff to about US$ 0.09 by mid 1993 with subsequent anumal increases. Table 5.4 shows the
projected average tariff over the year for 1993 to 1998. TANESCO raised its average tariff to US$ 0.07
in March 1993 and has agreed to raise it again to US$ 0.09 by mid year, as a condition of effectiveness
of the proposed credit (para 7.3(v)). These increases will reult in an average, over 1993, of about US$
0.08, as shown in Table 5.4. The value of US$ 0.09 per kWh is within the range of the LRMC which is
estimated to be between US$ 0.09 and US$ 0.10. The Tariff Study (Schedule 1) will establish a more
accurate estimate of LRMC and TANESCO has agreed to implement promptly the recommendations of
the study with respect both to structure and to level. TANESCO has also agreed that until it implements
the recommdations of the study, it will increase tariffs in US$ terms at 3.5 percent per annum, the
estimated US$ inflation rate.

5.25 The implementation of the above tariff adjustments, coupled with the projected growth in sales,
will result in adequate finds to finance at least 27 percent of TANESCO's capital investments from funds
which it will generate during the 1993-1998 period and yield an average rate of return on net revalued
fixed assets of about 5 perce during the project period. The projected operations of TANESCO indicate
that its future financial performance will be satisfactory with a projected debt service coverage varying
between 1.4 and 1.8 for this period. TANESCO has agreed that it will not incur any debt unless a
reasonable forecast of its revenues aud expenditures shows projected internal cash generation of at least
1.3 times the projected debt service requirement on all debt (para 7.2(iii)). Also, TANESCO agreed that
it would seek IDA's approval before nmking any additional investments in excess of US$ 5 million which
are not included in its investment program, and provide IDA with satisfactory evidence of the
investments' economic and technical viability (para 7.2(iv)).
5.26  Credit 1687 liiked TANESCO's financial performance to a 10 percent rate of return on average
revalued net fixed assets in operation. But because of the uncerty surrounding the current value of
TANESCO's assets and the funure value of the TSh, it is preferable to monitor TANESCO's future
financial performance to a covenant stipulatng an agreed level of cash generation. Therefore, according
to the agreme, TANESCO will generate sufficient cash to finance yearly, from inhernal sources, the
equivalent of not less than 16 percent for 1993, and 25 percent thereafter, of the average annual capital
expenditures incurred or expected to be incurred over a three year period, comprising the year in
question, and those immediately preceding and following such years. Furthernore, each year, no later
than 90 days prior to the end of the fiscal year, TANESCO has agreed to review, with IDA, whether
TANESCO will be able to meet the targets for internal cash generation. In the event such review showed
that TANESCO would not meet the requiement, TANESCO has agreed that it promptly will take all
necessary measures in order to meet such requirements, including increasing rates (para 7.2(vi)).

VI. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
6.1   Two demand forecasts form the basis of the least-cost analysis that justifies the project (pam
3.13). The base case forecast envisages an average annual energy generation inrease of 5.8 percent
through the year 2002, linked to a forecasted anmial GDP growth of about 4 pecent. The corresponding
forecast of peak demand is a increase from 297 MW in 1991 to 569 MW in 2002. The low growth
forecast envisages an average annual increase of ener-v generation of 4.1 percent, linked to an annual
GDP growth of 3 percent. The corresponding forecast of peak demand is an increase to 477 MW by
2u02. TANESCO's planning consutants, Acres Interational prepared the forecasts and an IDA appraisal
mission refined them further (para 3.1).
6.2 Acres International proposed a generation sequence, in 1990, with the Lower Kihansi plant
followmg the redevelopment of the Pangani hydro station. This sequence was modified in 1992. The
main change to the original generation sequence is the proposd ialation of a series of gas turbines of
about 50 to 60 MW and subsequen additions up to about 110 MW. As pam 3.2 notes, this addional
dtrmal generation will be necessary because of projected deficits in geneaion due to higher than
expected load growth, severe drought hydrology, equipme  failures, design defects, slow rehabilitation of
thermal plants, etc. This change also has intrduced great flexibility in the generation sequence both with
respect to energy and capacity. The gas turbines will allow better uwe of the available water by
permittng more freedom in the dispatch of the hydro plants. Since gas turbines have a relatively short
delivery period, TANESCO can respond quicldy to an increasing or decreasing rate of load growdt by
acceleraing(assuming financing is available) or deferring installation of some of these units. Also, in
determining the ultimate capacity of the gas turbines, TANESCO's consultants have experienced some
difficulty in predicting the firm energy capability of the hydro plants because of sparse data. Therefore,
TANESCO has arranged for increased data collection both in the context of this project and the Pangami
redevelopment project to overcome this difficulty.
6.3   The selection of Lower Kihansi as the next major geneaing plant in the least- cost sequence
resulted from a combined assesment of its economic viability and risk. The studies supporting the kast-
cost generation sequence showed that on the basis of discounted present worth costs, the Lower Kihansi
project and a termal plant using gas from the Songo Songo field were equal within the margin of error.
The Government and IDA concluded that the Songo Songo gas fired generation and the related gas-field
seemed more likely to attract private sector financing than a hydroelectric project. However, the
Government and IDA agreed that there were major risks with arrangig such financing, such as finding a
mechanism to ensure foreign investors of currency convertibility, and preparing an equitable operations
contract. Takdng all these factors into account, the Government and IDA agreed that the risk of not
meetng the commissioning date for required new capacity was too high for the gas-fired generation
pion given the importance of electricity to the economic recovery of the country.
6.4   The other components of the project that relate to TANESCO's investment program are
transmission and distrbtiion developments to complement the generation expansion and institutonal
measures to provide for the management of the system. The components which ZSFPC will implement
are needed to help bring urgent improvements to that company's commercial operations. The components
which MWEM wil implement include a sector study needed to direct development of the power sector
with private sector involvement, and developments of generation based on indigenous natural gas. The
development at Mnazi Bay is justified to replace the use of diesel fiuel. The promotion of the Songo



-66-
So  gas-field development is a logical step since it woud produce the fuel supply for an impot
option in the geeration expanion sequence.
Economic Internal Rate of Return for Conmonnts In TANESCO's  vsent Progam
6.5 The basis of the economic analysis for the proposed project is TANESCO's Investment Program
for 1992-1998, which the project appraisal mission reviewed. Since the proposed project is an integral
part of the 1992-1998 power system investment program, the analysis does not separate its costs and
benefits from the total costs and benefits of the investment program. The analysis calculates the
Economic Internal Rate of Return (EIRR) for the base case and does sensitivity analysis for five
alternative cases 12/. For the base case, the estimated EIRR is 15 percent. Since the basis of the
EIRR is the whole investment program, the economic return of 1S percent does not accwrately reflect the
economic return of the main investment under the project, i.e. Lower Kihansi. For the Lower Kihansi
scheme, including related transmission and distribution, the EIRR is about 20 percent.
Economic Cost of the Investment Proram
6.6   The economic costs comprise: (i) investment and re-investment costs, including physical
contingencies, required to meet incremental demand; and (ii) incremental operation and maintenae of
generation plants, and transmission and distnbution networks. However the economic costs do not include
investments in institutional development, training, studies, or investments to accommodate a firther 120
MW at Lower Kihansi in the future. Economic costs are expressed in mid 1992 values and in erm  of
local currency by converting foreign components and for domestic currency by convertng foreign
components of investments at the shadow exchange rate, equivalent to Tsh 400=US$1. Annex 6.1
provides further details of the economic costs used and the resulting cash flows.
Project Economic Benefits
6.7    The measurable economic benefits of the proposed project consist of increased energy sales made
possible by the proposed investments and increased supply reliability as a result of the distribution
network rehabilitation. The energy benefits, in the form of increased sales, increase through the 1990s as
the demand increases, and reach a plateau in 2002 when the combined icmental generating capability
of new plants has been reached. The analysis holds the energy benefits constant from 2002 to the end of
the investments' economic lifetime in the year 2031. The value of the energy benefits derives from the
evaluation of revenue benefits to TANESCO of the incremenal electrcity sales and an estimation of the
consumer surplus. The basis of the consumer surplus is a demand analysis which takes into account: (i)
the willingness of consumers to pay for electricity, relative to alternative energy sources such as kerosene
for lighting and captive diesel genemting units; and (ii) the observed cost of using the electricity
TANESCO suppliers, based on the company's 1992 marginal tariff for these consumers. Based on this
analysis, the willingness-to-pay for inremental electricity consists of: (i) Tsh 45, equivalent to US$0.113
JW/   The base case assumes the base case demand forecast and investment cost as detailed in
paras. 6.12-6.13. Four of the sensitivity tests are presented in Table 6.1 and the fifth in para.
6.17.



-67 -
for residentia consnmers; (ii) Tsh 55, equivalent to US$0. 138, for small commercial and iust
consumers; and (iii) Tsh 40, equialent to US$0.10 for large iustial and comercial consumers.
Annex 6.2 provides the details of the methodology used and the estmated value of benefits.
6.8   The proposed investmet in distibution network rehabilitation will result in benefits to the
economy in the form of improved supply reliability. Generally, these benefits to the economy are high
but their quanification is not easy. However, it is possible to estimate benefits from the esthnated
reductions in outages. According to the ESMAP Power Loss and Distribution Expansion Study, these
benefits would affect about 0.5 percent of the total sales between 1995 and 1997, and 1 percent of total
sales from 1998, the year of distribution rehabilitation completion. The following estimates for the
economic cost of outages provide an approximaon of the value of reliability improvemens: (i) US$1
per kWh for industrial and commercial consumers and US$0.5 per kWh for residental onumers of
unanticipated outages according to a TANESCO study in 1985; (ii) US$1.1 per kWh of unanticipated
outags  to all consume  according to Acres International in 1989; (iii) US$0.3-0.75 per kWh of
anticipated outages to all consm   by Acres International in 1992; and (iv) US$0.4-2.3 per kWh for
industria consumers of unanticipated outages according to an ESMAP survey in 1990, part of the Loss
Reduction and Distribution Expansion Study. These estimates illusrrate that the costs of outages vary
depending on whether the consumer expect it or not, and any suspension of economic activity that would
reslt. For the pmpose of this study, the estmated value of the economic benefits of reliability
Improvements is US$0.75 per kWh used.
6.9   In addition, the proposed p-oject will result in some benefits which are difficutt to quan*, some
of which the economic analysis consequently does not include. These benefits accrue from the
rehabilitation of the Kidatu hydro station. The proposed invesunent would make it possible to rehabilitate
the three S1 MW units in a planned manner in order to avoid the negative impacts that would be a result
of an abrupt breakdown of a unit, or in the worst case, the concurrent breakdown of all the three units.

6.10  The sensitivity of the economic internal rate of return was tested for the following four altemative
cases: (i) 20 percent increase in the capital cost of the investments and reinvestments (above the physical
contingency already included); (ii) low case demand forecast; (iii) one-year delay in the commissioning
of the Lower Kihansi scheme; and (iv) one-year delay in the commissioning of Lower Kihansi coupled
with 20 percent increase in capital cost of the investment program. The following table illustrates the

investment costs). Pinally, using the low demand forecast as a basis for benefits results in an EIRR of

Averzenremental Economic Cost of Electricity
6.12   An indication of an appropriate tariff level based on economic principles, is the average
ncremental economic cost (AIC) of electricity supply. Based on the costs developed above (para 6.12)
and the base case demand forecast, the AIC is estimated to be between US$0.09 and US$0.10 per kWh
(at 12 % discount rate) of which US$ 0.062 are attributable to the investment program and the balance to
the incremental operation, maintenance and fuel costs. TANESCO's average tariff in 1992 was about
US$ 0.06 per kWh but will increase to US$ 0.09 per kWh in 1993 as a result of the implementation of
the agreed tariff increases (para. 5.24). The tariff study, which the proposed project will provide will
recommend further improvements of the level and structure of the tariffs, including the possible use of a
fuel surcharge (para. 5.24).
Justification of Mnazi Bay Development
The main reasons for the Mnazi Bay development are: (i) to replace the use of imported diesel
fuel, which must be transported several hundred kilometers from Dar Es Salaam, with indigenous gas;
(ii) to improve the availability and capacity of supply to serve local industry and commerce; and (iii) to
demonstrate an approach to involving the private sector in power generation using gas, found in
conjunction with petroleum exploration, that would otherwise have no commercial use. Annex 6.3 shows
an analysis of the component based on replacing and supplementing the existing generation with gas
turbine generation using the gas. The equalizing discount rate for the scheme is about 30%. Chapter 4
discusses the way in which the private sector will be involved both in financing and subsequent
management of the scbeme (para 4.34).



VII. AGREEdENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1   The Government aareed during nefotiations to:
(i)    make the necessary arrangements to separate TANESCO's in-house business for
the construction of transmission and distribution lines from the rest of the
company and offer this business for sale to private investors by December 31,
1993 (para. 2.35);
(ii)   engage consultants, by January 1, 1994, to study privaization options, review
IDA's comments on the study, agree with IDA on a plan of action and implement
the resulting plan (para. 2.39);
(iii)  provide full financing and reimburse TANESCO for any rural electrificaton
project or other investment which it requires TANESCO to make, that the
Govemment considers socially inportant, but which is not financially viable
(para. 4.8);
(iv)   relend to TANESCO, US$180 million at an interest rate of at least 7.43 % over a
repayment period of 20 years, including a grace period of five years, with
TANESCO bearing the foreign exchange risk (para. 4.40);
(v)   relend to ZSFPC, US$ 2 million at an annual interest rate of at least 7.43% for a
repayment period of 20 years, including a grace period of five years, with ZSFPC
bearing the foreign exchange risk (para. 4.40);
(vi)  hold a mid-term review of the proposed project jointly with IDA and TANESCO
no later than June 30, 1996 (para. 4.67);
(vii) hold an interim review of the proposed project, no later than June 30, 1994, with the joint
participation of the Government, TANESCO, VDA and the cofinanciers, to examine the
progress made in meeting the key conditions designed to improve TANESCO's
performance and implemening a partil divestiture of its operations (para 4.68);
(viii) make direct payments to TANESCO on a quarterly basis, beginning September
30, 1993, for the electricity consumption of those consumers which, for
humanitarian reasons, the Government does not want TANESCO to disconnect
(para. 5.17);
(ix) ensure compliance with the tariff agreement between TANESCO and ZSFPC and
cover any shortfall to TANESCO due to non-payment by ZSFPC (para. 5.18);
and
(x)   have qualified, independent auditors acceptable to IDA audit the records and
accounts related to the proposed project, including the Project Account, the
Special Account and Statements of Expenditure, each fiscal year, and furnish IDA

witi a certified copy of the audit report no  ter h  six months after the end of
the fisal year (pam. 5. 10).
7.2   TANESCO ageed durg neoIations to:
(i)    have qualified, independent auditors acceptable to IDA ait records and accounts
related to the proposed project, including the Project Account, the Special
Account and the Statem  of Expenditures, each fiscal year, and furnih IDA
with a certified copy of te audit report within ten months of the 1992 fic  year
and within six months after the end of each successive fiscal year (para. 5.10);
(ii)   until the imple on of the agreed rm     dons of the tariff stdy adjust
its average tariff by 3.5% annually in US$ terms to offset the estmated
international inflation (para. 5.24);
(iii) not incur any debt unless a reasonable forecast of its revenues and expenditures
shows that the projected interal cash generation is at least 1.3 times the estimated
debt service requirem  on all debt (pam 5.26);
(iv) review its investment program with IDA anmually, incorporate changes acceptable
to IDA and, before making any investment in excess of US$5 million not included
in its investment program, provide IDA with satisfactory evidence of the
investment's economic and technical viability (para 4.8);
(v) not change the agreed Statement of Policy without IDA's approval (para. 2.6);
(vi)  generate, on a fiscal year basis, funds from internal sources equivalent to not less
than 15% for 1993, 25% for 1994, 20% for 1995 and 30% for 1996, 1997 and
1998, of the average amnnal capital expenditures incwred or expected to be
incurred for that year and the next three fiscal years. Also, before the end of
September in each of its fiscal years, TANESCO will, on the basis of forecasts
acceptable to IDA, review the etent to which it has met the above rquimnents
and if the review shows that TANESCO would not meet the he mreuim ,
TANESCO and the Govermnent would prompty take all necessay measures in
order to meet such requirments (para. 5.25); and
(vii)  execute the Implemention Program outlined in Schedule 1.
7.3    Conditons of Effctiveness
(i)    The Government and TANESCO will execute a subsidiary loan      (pan 4.40)
(ii)   The Goverimnent will make satisfactory progress in finalizing co-financing arrangements
for the project (para 4.40).



- 71 -
(iii) TANESCO will engage an externally recruited project manage for the lower Kihansi
hydropower scheme, whose qualifications and terms and conditions of employment will be
acceptable to IDA (para. 4.42).
(iv)   TANESCO will establish a project mnagement unit and appoint a Project Manager with
qualifications and experience accptable to the IDA (para. 4.41)
(v)   TANESCO will have completed the certification of bills for outstanding debts of
Government Agencies and the Government will have converted promissary notes, related
to these debts, into cash (para 5.15).
(vi)   There will be an increase in TANESCO's average tariff to the equivalent of US$
0.09 per kWh (para 5.24).
7.4    2ondition of Disbursement for the ZSFPC Portion
(i)    ZSFPC shall have concluded a satsifactory subsidiary loan agreement and adjustd its
tariffs in line with those on the mainland (5.18).
7.5   R.ecommendto
With the above agreements, the Project is suitable for an IDA credit of SDR144.2 million
(US$200 million equivalent) to the Governmeat of Tanzania at standard terms, with maturity of 40 years
luding 10 years grace.



Schedule I
TANZANIA
POWE1R  PROJECI
Imp1emeXtation Progrm
1.    TANESCO will, by December 31. 1993, prepare a demand side management program acceptable
to IDA.
2.    TANESCO, will continue to fill the positions of the Managing Director and the Deputy Managing
Directors with qualified and experienced staff satisfactory to IDA.
3.    TANESCO will carry out a survey of salaries paid by other conmercial organizations in Tanzania
and based on the survey establish a competitive compensation structure by January 1, 1994 for all levels
of its employees.
4.    Beginning on July 1, 1994 and thereafter on each anniversary date, TANESCO will prepare an
Anmual Corporate Plan, acceptable to IDA, for the next financial year, establishing medium-term S year
business objectives and fncial projections as well as an annual budget.
-.    By January 1, 1994, TANESCO will appoint the following staff with qualifications and
experience and on terms and conitions of employment acceptable to IDA; Planning Manager, Systems
Manager, Management Training and Personnel Expert, Director and Head Instructor of the Morogoro
Training institute, Training Expert for Kidatu Technical Training Institute, Training Expert in
Distribution Planning and a PIP Expert.
6.    TANESCO will (a) ewne an exernally recruited manpower expert for about 6 months to carry
out a study of its manpower requirements; (b) review the results of the study with IDA by December 31,
1993; and (c) by June 30, 1994, implement recommendation of the study, taking IDA's comments into
account. Until it has started to implement the recommendations of the study, TANESCO will maintain a
ureeze on employment, except to fill key vacant management and technical positions, and to fulfill special
needs.
7.    TANESCO will establish a project management unit and appoint a Project Manager who will have
the following responsibilities: (a) coordinatng the execution of the Project; (b) liaising with IDA and
other financiers on the various components of the Project; (c) dealing with all correspondence,
procurement activities, progress reports and other project related matters; and (d) liaising with consultants
employed to supervise Lower Kiliansi hydroelectric plant, Distribution Works and rehabilitation of the
Kidatu Hydroelectric station.
8.    TANESCO will by June 30, 1994, implement an Environmental Mitigation Plan for Lower
Kihansi Scheme and the transmission line from Lower Kihansi to Iringa and from Lower Kihansi to
Kidatu.
9.    TANESCO will: (a) by June 30, 1994 carry out an Asset Revaluation Study under the terms of
reference satisfactory to the Association; (b) furnish the draft recommendations of the study to IDA for its
review and comments; (c) finalize the recommendations taking IDA's comments into account; (d)
implement the recommendations of the study within 6 months of the study's completion; and (e) anuaily
thereafter revalue its assets according to the methodology recommended in the study.



-74-
10.   TANESCO will: (a) diligently enforce a detailed plan of action acceptable to IDA for recovery of
overdue accounts from Government agencies and parastatls, as well as its policy of disconnecting
delinquent private consumers; (b) continue to prepare every quarter for IDA's review, a report
summarizing its outstanding consumer accounts; and (c) by December 31, 1994, reduce its accounts
receivable to no more than sixty days of sales and maintain it at no higher than that level thereafter.
11.   TANESCO will: (a) by June 30, 1994, carry out a Tariff Study under terms of reference
satisfactory to IDA; (b) funish the study to IDA for its review and comments; (c) finalize the
recommendations of the study taking IDA's comments into account; (d) within six months of its
completion implement the recommendations of the study; and (e) update thereafter the tariffs regularly
based on the methodology proposei in the study.
12.   To ensure the continued safety of the dam and strictures, no later than one year before the
expected completion of the dam and structures, TANESCO would provide IDA with details of its
arrangements for inspecting and mantining the dam and associated structures.

The figure shows tfiat the mission's base case forecast is slightly higiher than the long-term
trend (based on 1980-1990 demand growth), while the low case forecast is somewhat lower. The
short term trnd is based on the demand growth during 1985- 1990 and, if continued, would
double the load every seven years. The current high residential demand growth has been mainly
an outcome of the increased consumption/consumer due to catching up in the demand of electrical
appliances, that have become more easily available in the aftermath of the economic recovery.
Such a rapid growth is not likely to persist in a longer-term however; anticipated increases in
tariff rates to more economic levels and the connection of lower income households as the
network expands, will probably slow down the growth. The growth in the industrial demand has
been largely in response to the GDP growth but the relatively high electricity demand to GDP
elasticities should decrease with the introduction of more efficient technology and higher capacity
utiization.

TANES%O's system planning criea of a 3 1/3 perce  risk of failure to meet system demand

2.   The projet is located in southwestern Tanzania, about 440 km fhom Dar es Salaam. Tbe
powerhouse site can be reached by road from Dar es Salaam via Mikumi and Ifakara, a road distance of
about 560 km. The TAZARA railway also passes within five kilomets of the powerhouse site 2but the
nearest station is at Chita which is some 17 km from the site.
3.   The project utlizes the flow of the Klhansi River, which has its source on the Central Plateau at
about 2100 m above sea level. T'e river drains generally southward toward the Uzungwa Scarp, falling
about 430 m in the fi 40 km, 350 m in the next 10 km, and 1000 m in the remaining five kilometers to
the foot of the scap, where it floWs into the Kilombero River. The Kilombero merges with the Great
Ruaha and drains into the Rufiji River system which flows into the sea about 140 km south of Dar es
Saam.
4.   Ihe proposed project consists of a low dam, at the head of the steepest section of the scarp,
divertng flow through a power tunnel, to an underground powehouse, and then back into the river at the
foot of the escarpment. A head of 851 m is developed over a total conduit length of 5.4 km.
5. There are three runoff gauging stations in uie Kihansi Basin, with the long- -eriod of records
tending back to 1975. EPDC, consultants for the feasibility study, extended the flow series back to
1957 by correlation with flow records for the adjacent Mpsnga River to the west of Kihansi. Since trends
for East Africa as a whole indicate long period cycles, with a relatively dry period prior to the mid
1950s, the series were further extended back to 1927 using tank model and rainfall records. A 61 year
synthetic flow series was therefore derived. This long term series confims a long period cyclic change in
1961 Ath the eatdier period relatively drier than the lat period. Preliminary checks of the EPDC
hydrology were carried out by NORPLAN, the consultas for the detailed design. They were in genera
agement with EPDC estimates, but considered them likely to be slightly high in the wet months, which
i8 of little consequence, since Kihansi will generally be spilling in those months. Hydrological studies,
including site gauging are continuing, and esdmates wil be continuously refined during project
construction.
6.   The average anmnal rainfal of the Kihansi Basin is esdmated at about 1500 mm. Average annual
flow at the damsite is estimated at about 16.3 m'Is, which repres  an anual runoff from the catchment
of some 870 mm. The tD months of highest flow are March to May, with total runoff in this period,
accounting for 50 to 60 percent of anmnu runoff. The three months of lowest flow are September to
November, with runoff in this period accounting for 10 to 15 percent of flow volume. The coefficient of
vaiation for mean monthly flows is high (30 to 40 percent) for December to June, and around 20 percent
for remaining moAths. About 4 to 5 points of this coefficient of variation are attributed to the long term
cyclic cange that occuTred in 1961.



- 97 -
Anx4.3
Page 4 of 14
7. The project Is located in a region known as the Tanzanian Shield, characterized by Precambrian
ipeous and metamorphic rocks, which have been subjected to tectonic movements, resulting in sesmic
actvity and block ftting including the formaton of the African Rif system.
8.   Ihe assement of site geology is based on geological surface mapping, about 4,075 m of seismic
profiling, and 1,400 m of diamond drilling. Bedrock over the entire site of structures and unnels,
consists of precambrian gndesses, with biotite gneiss predominating, and with quartz gneiss, amphibolite
and lamprophyre existing in 'me areas. Weathering depth is variable, but typically ranges from 15 to 20
m. In part, particularly at the bottom of the escarpment, the weathering profile is overlaid by slope
wasb and alluvium. In the bedrock, a distinct foliation can be observed dipping 20 to 45 degrees to the
WNW. Ihe main joint orientation strikes in the E-W direcion. Faults striking NE-SW and N-S also
occur.
9.   The conclusion from the seismic profiling and the drilling is that the rock conditions are expected
to be favorable for uneling with a high rock strength and a favorable orientation of main discontinuities
in relation to tuunels and the power station. f tectonic origin. In general, the rock is suitable for unlined
drill and blast tunnels, with rock bolting and shotcreting providing permanent support. Crossing of faults
may require heavy permanent support, such as thick steel fibre reinforced shotcrete combined with
prouted rock bolts, and in serious cases bar reinforced arches. Over the route of the pressure tunnel and
shaft, support requirements are conservatively assessed as follows: minimum or no support for 64 percent
of the length, systematic bolting and shotcrete for 23 percent of the lengul, and relatively heavy suppr t
for the remaining 13 percent. The tailrace tunnel is expected to require slightly more support on average.
oJect Qdig
Q=cdt SOta
10. The first serious hydro-planning studies for the Kihansi River were carried out by Norconsult in
1983-84, as part of the "Ruflji Basin Hydropower Master Plan" fimded by NORAD. The development
conceived at that time was a two-step development, with the upper step providing storage, and developing
a gross head of 240 m, and the lower run-of-river step developing the remaining 1010 m of head. In
1985, Acres as a part of the Power Sector Development Plan studies, re-evaluated the cascade
configuration and suggested moving both upper and lower dams downstream, the upper step developing
228 m and lower step 798 m. EPDC carried out a full feasibility study in 1989190, and as a part of this
study re-considored the staging question. Based on their anatyses, they basically agreed with Acres, but
detailed optmiation of dam locations and heights resudted in the upper and lower steps developing 214.5
m and 813 m respectively. The EPDC studies showed the Upper Kihansi scheme to be only marginally
economic, even when attibuting to it the fWl benefit of flrming up the energy in Lower Kihazsi. Acres
in the 1990 itudies into the use of gas in the power sector, noted that this latter benefit was probably
overestimated, since considerable firming up of Lower ICihansi will occur in any case when
complementary operation with Mtera-Kidatu and the large Mtera storage is considered. They therefore
suggested that the cascade configuration, particularly the location of the Upper Kihansi storage be
reconsidered.



- 98 -
Anex 4.3
Page S of 14
11. This re-consideration was included as part of the first stage of the detailed design studies, carried
out by NORPLAN. They evaluated the overall development options, and conc2uded that two alternative
developments of the cascade were equally attractive within the accuracy of the exercise. Both alternatives
included an upper dam at the original NORCONSULT site, and both had the lower powerhouse at the
foot of the escarpment. Ahey varied only In the location of the intermediate powerhouse and dam.
12. The first had the lower dam at the EPDC site and developed heads of 41E m and 847 m. The
second step is therefore that which had been studied by EPDC to feasibility level. The second option put
the lower dam at the originl NORCONSULT site and develnved heads of 255 m and 1,010m. The first
option was recommended by NORPLAN and chosen by TANESCO based on the following factors: lTe
two steps development is more balanced, taking into account considerations such as construction time of
first step (and associated interest during construction and earliest in-service date), investment
requirements, and the time for system absorption of first step energy.
-     hMe lower step for the first option was already at full feasiblity level, whereas for the
second option, at least six months would -e required to confirm feasibility.
-    Technical factors associated with the 1010 m development, which is approaching the level of
precedence, could Increase risk and maintenance requirements.
Power Conduit Conffiggation
13. During the various study stages, numerous power conduit configurations were examined.
NORCONSULT envisaged a right bank tunoel and underground powerhouse, with the tairace tunnel
passing under the Kihansi River to exit on the left bank at the foot of the scarp. Acres, in moving the
lower damsite downstream, moved the conduit to the left bank which appeared to be topographically more
logical for this damsite. On the basis of 1 in 50,000 mapping only, they proposed an open canal, leading
to a surface penstock and surface powerhouse. EPDC, on the basis of more detaied mapping and site
investigations reverted to an underground scheme for the power conduit. For the powerhouse, after
examining both underground and semi-underground solutions they opted for a semi-underground
anngemenL
14. NORPLAN, as part of the first stage of the detailed design studies, carried out a preliminary
examination of 16 left and right bank alternatives. This confirmed the left bank location to be better and
the four alternatives on this bank were given priority in a preliminary site investigation program,
consisting of geological mapping and seismic refraction profiling. These investigations indicated that a
primary controlling factor was the distance that the tailrace channel would have to pass through the highly
fractured zone at the foot of the escarpment, and the associated excavation depths. This factor reduced
the options to two quite close alignments which were investigated in detail before selecting the final
alignmenL The selected alignment places the tunnel further into the hillside than the EPDC alternative,
which also has the advantage that insitu stresses in the rock will be greater than internal water pressure in
the tunnel, allowing structura linings to be generally eliminated, without danger of rock failure by
hydraulic fracture.

IS.  lstlled capacity of Lower Kihansi was chosen based on a capacity and energy balance of the
overall system after the full commissioning of Lower Kihansi.
16.  The firm and aerage enaergies for the . tall hydro system, before and after the introduction of
Lower Kibansi were derived using the HEC S . servoir simulation program. Thbis program was used to
optiize the coleme y opion of Lower Kihansi with the existing system, primarly the large
storag of the Mtera/Kidatu cascade. Th genera system cf opeaton is tat In high flow months
Kihansi, with daily storage only, oprates on base load, keeping spill to a minirmum, while the
MteraK1datu cascade operates to meet the peak, with excess inflows stored in its large reservoir. In dry
monfts the duties are reversed wift Kihansi using its limied inflow to meet peak loads, with
Uter/Kldatn operating on base, supplementing its inflows by withdrawals from storage.
17.  These reservoir simulation studies indicated that firm energy esimates of the existing hydro system
are somewhat sensitive to assumptions concerning evaporation from the large Mtera reservoir. Site
measurements during 1992 indicate that orWinal estimates made by SWECO may have bee- eonservative,
and laterst by ACRES even more conservative. An intensive program of measuremw   has been
put in motion to refine thest esimates. Pending the completion of the program, upper and lower bound
simates of hydro energ generation for the system before and after Lower Kini were derived. The
ct of the pible later upstream addition of Upper Kihansi was also examined.
18.  Firm hydro energy in the design dry year was assumed to be supplemented by therma energy from
41 MW of new or recenty rehabilitated thermal units, together with 60 MW (the minimum that is likely
to be instaled) of gas turbine capacity sdheduled to be installed before Lower Kihansi. These thermal
units were assumed to operae at a maximum 75 percent capacity factor during the design dry year and on
standby only during an average hydrological year. The derivation of the instaled capacity required at
Kihansi to bring the system into balce, after istallation of Lower Kihansi is shown in the following
table:

increase to 271 to 324 MW. Up to about 300 MW installed, the incremena cost of capacity is quite low

also means that installation of some units can be delayed, while making initial low cost provisions towards
an ultimate installation.
20.  Taking the above into account, together with the maximum sized unit consistent with the size of the
TANESCO system, an initial installation of three 60 MW units was chosen, with initial provisions made
for later addition of up to two more unit. Taking into account retrement of old diesel plant, the initial
installation could be filly absorbed by the system within about four years of commissioning.
21. Even if Upper Kihansi is not constucted in the medium term, or if hopes of low evaporation at
Mtera are not realized, the additional generatng units, with their very low capacity cost are still likely to
be  stalled at some dm  in the future, in paraliel with base load hydro or thermal plant, with Lower
Ki i taking up more of a peaking role.
DESCRION OF SCHSENM ELEM4ENI
Dam and IntaLe
22. Two alternative damsites 300 m apart, both identified by EPDC, were investigated by NORPLAN
before choosing the upper site, based on both technical and economic considerations. The height of the
dam crest above river bed level is about 23m, with a 25m maximum height above foundation level.
Normal and minimum operatng levels are at Elevations 1144 m and 1138 m respectively. This provides
live storage of some 870,000 m& which is sufficient to provide for full daily regulation of flow for an
ultimate installed capaciy of 300 MW under the most adverse inflow and outflow conditions. Dead
storage provided is 280,000 e3, which is conservatively estimated to provide at least 50 years of sediment
storage. However, becuse of uncertainty of sedimentation estimates, sediment flushing facilities are
provided, and periodic flushing of sediment, during periods of very high flow, is assumed.
23. A concrete dam, incorporating spilway, stilling basin and sediment flushing facilities is provided.
Ihe design discharge of the spillway is 470 m3/s.
Pwr Intake. Shaft   Presure Tunnel
24. IThe headrace italke is located adjacent to the dam on the left side of the river. TMe weir siUl is at
Elevation 1133 m. The intake is protected by trashracks, a sliding maintenance gate and a roller mounted
closure gate.
25.   Ihe intake is connected to the headrace unnel by a verticai shaft 240 m long and 25 ni in area,
sunk or raise bored from the Intake site. The headrace tunnel is 2900 m long and 30 m2 in area, with an
inclination of I in 5.0, the maximum for wheeled transport. The tunnel is slightly oversized in
comparison with that which would have been provided from the viewpoint of head loss. This provides
regulating stability without the need for any separate surge or oscillation damping shaft. At the bottom of
the steep inclined muel is a sandtrap for bedload followed by a 100 m long steel lined section leading to
the powerhouse. The intake shaft and tunnel are generally unlined, with bolting and shotcreting providing



- 101 -
Annex 4.3
Page 8 of 14
the main permanent support. Heavier support ad probably conre fitig Is expected over short
sections totalling 13 percent of the length.
Powerbouse ad Tailrace
26.  The underground powerhouse has a cavern 95.5 m long (this includes space for future units), 12.6
m wide and 30 m high, supported by systematic bolting and shotcreting. Transformers are located in a
separate cavern.
27.  From the powerhouse, bidividual outlet unnels fom each turbine, merge int a 1500 m long 36 in2
free flow tunnel, which in turn leads into an 800 m long canal, discharging back into the Kihansi River.
'lmmia and EipeeSalE
28. The powerhouse will ultimately accommodate fif-e (initWily three) Pelton turbines, each developing
60 MW, with a power flow of 8.3 m3/s, under a rated e. of 845 m. he unts are of the veical type,
with four jets, and rotatng at a speed of 600 tpm. Vertical Qynchronous generators, rated at 70.5 MVA
at 0.85 power factor, are of convendonal design with upper guide bearing and lower combined guide and
thrur bearing, with mechanical braking and a hydralic rotor Jacking system.
29. Isophase buses and generator circuit breakers are provided between the generators and three phase
transformers located in a separate cavern. The high voltage side of the step up transformers will be
connectedto the outdoor 220 kV switchgear via 220 kV single phase oil filled or XLPE cables.
Az=es and Infisrcure
30.  Most heavy equipment will be tasported to the project site by rail to MlWuba station 24 km south
. !Kiansi. The road from Dar es Salaamn will still be used for personnel and light transport, and the
section from IfWakaa to Kihansi wil be upgraded as part of the project. A 17 km long paved two lane
road up the escarpment to the damsite will also be constucted as part of the project. The construction
camp will be located on an existing road just south of the powerhouse. The camp will house all
employees and workers, with about 70 staff houses plus worker accommodation. It will develop into a
self contained village with up to 1700 persons during constuction, and with all basic services from shops
and school to bank and police station.
Opnion of Panel of Exprt
The panel of experts for the Kihansi project met in February 1992 to review the project. The
following are the Project Overview and the Closure from the panel's repotr
Poecta Overviff
As prenty designed the Lower Kihansi Hydropower Project consists of:
- a relatively small dam of heigbt 25m (HRWL = 1144 MASL)



- 102 -
Annex 4.
Page 9 of 14
a 240 m long headrace shaft (vertical)
a 2740 m long Inclined tunnel
a 125m long steel lined penstock with two bifurcatons feeding water to
3 x 60 MV pelton turbines hcused in
a 14m span cavern located a depth of 575 m
a 1500 m long tarace tunnel
-    infrstructure and an access road
various appurtenart openings and structures, including a tailrace channel and a 1100 m
access tunnel to the power station complex.
The Panel considers that the general concept of the Ptoject represents an excellent solution,
afbrding the greatest possible advantage of the geologic, hydrologic and topographic
conditios of the area. The difficulty of access to and within the site appear to have been
well considered in the optimization of the project construction. With respect to the project's
opational characteristics, these have been adequately matched and dimensioned to fulfil the
immediate fictional requirements of the nations system, whilst providing for flexibility for
futu  adptation
Within the perspecdve of the national system, the time to commissioning is critical and a
significant consideration in the cost optimization. In this respect, the focus on the Lower
KJhansi as the project for first implementation is appropriate. With only modmrate variations
in the seasonal flow of the upper reaches of the Kihansi River, a small reservoir is possible.
The steepness of the terrain virtally eliminates the need for a long low pressure waterway,
and the high head of 850 m furtb" enhances the energy capability relative to the
constructon cost.
When considered within the spectrum of hydropower projects worldwide, the rock conditions
do present a generaly favourable combinations of circumstaces for the timely and cost
dective construction of the underground works. Individual design features requiring special
atenion are deted in the remaining sections of this review.
Although alternaive proposals for the staged development of the Kihansi river have not been
reviewed in detail, the time for additional investigation the achieve a commensurate
definition of feasibility is judged a significant cost disadvantage to the system economics.
The presendy proposed development of Lower Kihansi mainai  flexibility in tie future
development and optimization of power generation and water storage.
Ihe balance of frm energy and capacity and the decision to adopt 3 x 60 MW units are
judged appropi   to the system requir_emes. Wih its high head and consequent ability to



- 103 -
Page 10 of 14
provide extra peaking cap4city at a relatively low cost, the provision for expansion to 300
MW is justified.
The following secdons offer commentts, suggestions and recommendations with respect to
design details, which when taken Into consideration would appear to permit on improvement
In the economics of construction and operation of the scheme.
It is concluded that Norplan's contribution to the optimization of the lower Kihansi project is well
directed and in so far as it has defined the key design parameters, the Panel considers the optimization
adequate at this stage.
'Me Panel agrmj with the general approach to the investigation, design dand construction of the
project. The topography and subsurface conditions favour a limited investigation, supported by and
integrated with two key elements of project realization:
-    a properly executed strategy for decision making and detailed design of a number of
elements duinn construction.
-    a flexible and fair contual philosophy for the main civil construction.
The main thrust of the Panel's concerns relates to a realistic targeting of the construction cost and
duration and the need to define more explicWy how the outstanding design deail will be undertaken,
prior to and during construeton. Considering the level of uncerinty in subsurface conditions, the
schedule for construction is perhaps slightly optimistic. Except for those dminatios which will be
completed during construction (refinement of power house location and orientation and length of steel
penstock), the layout and dimensioning of the scheme is considered to be essentially optimal.
Throughout this review, nuerous comments and recommendations have been made with the
objecdve of pointing to possible areas in which design detail may be improved and the construction
process made more responsive to achieving a quality product. Some minor adjustments to key design
parameters have also been suggested but in all cases, no major cost increments are identified, nor is any
major procurement delay anoicipated.
It is anticipated dtat in response to this review, the Project Defition Report will be updated,
reviewed and re-issued, and included in the solicitation for tenderers for the main civil contract The
Panel expects to effect a detafled review of the technical specifications prior to their reles. Review will
also be required of the schedule for solicitation of the interated contracts, including a review of the
tecnical pecifications for the mechanical/electrical equipment.
Based on the curt procurement schedule and progamme of documentadon, it would appear most
opporue for the Panel to meet and review during August 1993.

B.   Ditribution MWIrk
31.  The load growth of about 10% over the last S years has resulted in a distribution system of
relatively high losses and low reliability. While the load growth is scattered geographically, some areas
may be lightly loaded, and others seriously overloaded. Carefilly planned works are required to reduce
losses and improve reliability resulting from insufficient maintenance and overloading of equipment.
During the last five years, JICA and IDA have both financed major rehabilitation projects, and
TANESCO's distribution system benefitted greatly from these projects, without which the load additions
from 1986 to date would not have been possible. However, the serving of the suppressed demand and the
addition of new loads have once again reduced many parts of the network to poor reliability and high
losses.
32. Previously, because of a shortage of foreign exchange, TANESCO made no serious effort to
prepare comprehensive development proposals, or to collect the data needed to prepare such proposals.
In 1990, ESMAP helped TANESCO establish a planning unit and provided state-of-the-art technology for
detailed distribution planning. ESMAP also helped TANESCO prepare the distribution component of the
proposed project. The component's economic benefits will be realized from three categories of work.
(i)  Loss reduction and improvement of reliability. The economic benefits of loss reduction
increase substantially in areas of high load growth because line losses increase each year in
proportion to the square of the load growth, and the reliability benefits can be computed on
the basis of an estimation of the savings in outages as a result of the ability to feed sections
of a network with more than one source.
(ii) Rehabiitaton of existing networks. The rehabilitation, as a result of insufficient
mintenance and natural ageing, is mandatory for TANESCO, which has an obligation to
continue to supply existing consumers. The economic value is in maintaining the assets of
the company and its ability to continue earning revenue.
(iii) Expansion of the networks Into new areas. The economic benefit of proposed expansions are
computed on the basis of LRMC and those that appear to be profitable in the near future, will
be constructed.
33. TANESCO, with ESMAP's help, has completed the proposed development program for Dar es
Salaam, Tanga, Moshi, and Arusha in sufficient detail that engineering design and the preparation of bid
documents can begin as soon as funds become available to hire consultants to carry out the work. The
new extensions to be financed by IDA wiMl require construction of about 128 kn of 33kV and I11WV lines,
77km of low voltage distribution lines, and the erection of about 176 distribution transformers. The
desip of the system is based on the current distrbution standards used in Tanzania. The cost per
customer connected of the proposed extensions is about US$795. It is conceivable that this could be
reduced to about US$500 to US$660 per customer if more appropriate standards are used. Because of the
urgency of the work, however, the project will be planned using the existing standards. TANESCO plans
to have a specialst review its standards as it sxpands Its distribution planning capability. If, as is likely,
new standards are adopted before all of the material is bought, the consultants naging the distribution
expansion work will be required to adopt the new standards.

C.   Workshops
34. The need for proper workshop facilities for any power utility is evident, especially in the power
stations. Unfortuately appropriate tools and equipment were not provided in the initial construction of
the power stations. Even Pangani, which is now under construction, has no provision for workshop
facilites. TANESCO, wI - te help and advice of consulting engineers, has prepared plans to build and
equip workshops in the l fen r' nes, and at the thre"  ajor powerhouses, to facilitate equipment repairs.
Each workshop would c e;4$,,d with the machim. - hand tools necessary to carry out the type of
repair envisioned at that -4r  on.
D.   Studies
35. Power Sector Master Plan. The last time a full power sector master plan study was done is in
1985. That study was updated on several occasions, as is customary, to reflect changing circumstances.
The accuracy of the study fails off every time it is updated and it is now necessary to perform a full
study, especially to re-define the load forecast model and to introduce new candidate plants in the
expansion sequence. Ihe new candidate plants will be defined by feasibility studies described later in this
paragraph. Since an update of the forecast model is urgent, the master plan study will be done in two
parts: the first to update the forecast model and derive a sequence based on the known candidate plants;
and the second, to incorporate the new candidate plants and the potential impact of demand side
management once the associated studies are completed. Future  jectL Tanzania has an abundance of
potential hydro sites. The project includes a study to evaluate four siues on the Masigira and Rumakali
rivers, and bring the two most promising sites to feasibility level. Asset Revaluation. TANESCO has
been revaluing its assets by using indices. This is normal but afer some years it is necessary to perform
a full inventory and valuation to check the data base. ODA has agreed to finance the necessary work.
IDA will comment on the terms of reference and the final report. Moreover, the project coins
provisions for a computeried asset register that will help TANESCO keep track of its assets once the
study is completed. Tariff Study . Although the level of TANESCO's tariffs has improved the strur
need revision to ensure that it promotes efficient use of electricity. Also, with the introduction of more
thermal generation that will be used if hydrology is poor, a fuel adjustment clause may be appropriate.
Therefore ODA has agreed to finance a tariff study to correct these deficiencies.
E.   Generation at Mnazi Bay
36. The Mnazi Bay scheme is a pilot operation to demonstrate the use of gas from remote gas wells in
the local economy, and at the same time test ways of involving both the finacing capability and
mangement inputs of the private sector in the early stages of a country's development. The scheme
comprises the workover and instllation of production facilities to an existing natural gas well,
construction of a 40km pipeline (8^ diameter to allow for future expansion), and installation of three
3MW gas turbines. The entire project will be engineered and built by a private company which will
operate the facility and sell power against a sales contract to TANESCO for distribution in the adjacent
towns. The Government will finance the pipeline and gas turbines and lease them back to the private
company. The private company will be required to prepare two turnkey contracts suitable for
hnternational Competitive Bidding, one for the pipeline and the other for the gas turbine facility. These
conacs will be financed by IDA. The company will make Its own arrangements for the gas production
facility which it will finance. The private company will be required to obtain any environmental
cleaances required. The Private company will be selected, after prequalification, by bidding on a



- 106 -
Annex 4,3
Page 13 of 14
contract prepared by a Consultant employed by the Governent. The Bidder offering the lowest
electricity price against an indexed contract, that will be included as part of the bidding documents, will
be awarded the contract.
F.   Demand Side Maagm
37.  ghlnA and &CkgWeu. lie objective of the ESMAP assistance !s to help TANESCO and the
Department of Energy (DOE) of the Ministry of Water, Energy and Minerals to prepare a medium-term
electricity demand-management straegy and implementation plan. Successful implementation of demand-
management measures, however, requires a supportive environment including: electricity tariffs that
reflect the real cost of supply; and a reasonably efficient and reliable supply system.
38. TANESCO and the Government have started increasing tariffs to levels that reflect the economic
cost of supply, and have recenty agreed to increase the average tariff to reflect the estimated LRMC by
mid 1993. Already, large industrW customers pay rates close to the real cost of the supply.
39.  The development of the demand-management strateg requires two phases: (a) analysis of cost and
benefits of various demand management measures and identification of the most cost-effective ones; and
(b) formulation of a comprehensive implemetation strategy.
40. Identflcion of DSM Mear. Since non-residential customers account for more than 50 percent
of TANESCO's total electricity sales and contrbute significantly to the mid-&y and evening peaks, the
project will focus on these customers. The main electricity consuming applications in the large
commercial bui)dings are lighting and air-conditioning while a significant portion of electricity consumed
by industries is acounted for by electrically powered motor drives, i.e. electrical motors that drive
compressors, pumps, fans and conveyor belts. Public lighting accounts for only less than a percent of
total electricity consumption but contributes to the evening peak. lbe National Urban Water Authority
(NUWA) accouts for about 5 percent of total electricity consumption and has an impact, especially, on
local peak loads.
41. Currently there are about 540 large commercial and industrial consumers in Tanzania, not
including public lighting and the NUWA. Of these about 30, repreenting different kinds of end-uses,
will be selected for a detailed inspection of their electricity consumption patterns based on reviews of
available load data and interviews. Energy audits to identify improvements will be undertaken at the
premises of the customers considered to have the most promising DSM potential when compared to
TANESCOs load shape objectives. Also, the results of the audits will be used to estimate the total
energy efficiency potental in a certain industria or commercial enterprise. The costs and benefits of
each mease wil be evaluated and the measures ranked accoding to electricity savings potential and
cost-effectiveness. In addition, the overall impact in Ums of monetary savings to industry, economic
benft to the country as a resut of reduction of electricity demand growth, and financial implications to
TANESCO wil be assessed. this analysis will also involve the estimation of investments required in
efflciency improving measures, to be used in the next least-cost power sector expansion plan. The
straegy will primarily target the following measures: (a) load management; () end-use efficiency; and (c)
power factor improvement.
42.  Formaon of an IMaementation Stratgy. The second phase of the strategy development is
concerned with implementg the identified efficiency improvement measures. The possible set of



_ 107 -
Annex 43
Page 14 of 14
measures include an appropriate electricity pricing policy; a complementary set of non-pricing incentives;
marketing and information campaigns; and an appropriate institutional framework to facilitate
collaborative action by all stakeholders: the customers, TANESCO, DOE, equipment manufactures and
suppliers, as well as energy service and financial intermediaries.
43.  EollowI  IuluniMatIni. Investments required to implement demonson projects are included
in the proposed project. The demonstrations would include a range of measures in industial, commercial
and public buildings. Fi        will be required for the following: (a) energy efficiency investments in
industrial and commercial enterprises for energy efficient equipment, retrofits, capacitors, etc.; (b) energy
efficiency investments in the public sector such as installation of energy efficient street lights and energy
efficiency improvements in water pump installations; (c) investments for TANESCO in dual tariff meters
for a limited number of large consumers, information campaigns, and installation of energy efficient
equipment in TANESCO's headquarters for demonstration purposes. (d) seminars and workshops; and (e)
euation of results.

POWE VI PROJECT
Monitoring Guidelines
Sanesco's operating performance has been monitored for some time
against selected indicators prepared by Tsanesco for all its seven sons,.
its two major power plants (Kidatu and Mtera) and headquarters as
consolidated and updated in Annex 2.2.  These performance  indicators would
contlnue to be monitored during the implementation of tte proposed project.
In addition, a number of target dates have been established to ensure
smooth  implementation of the proposed project               Summarized
below are some of the areas which in particular would be closely monitored
by IDA during the izplemmetation of the proposed project:
(a) preparation of TOR, Invitation to bid and contract award
for consultants;
(b) issue of bid documents, bid closing, and contract award;
(c) progress of construction and installation of equipment;
(d) project cost estimates and financing;
(e)  electricity sales and number of connections by categories;
(f) electricity generat4in (hydro and thermal);
(g) system losses as Z of generation (technical and non-
technical);
(h) firm capacity and generation capability:
(i)  planned and unplanned outages and their 4uration for power
plants, transmission and distribution systems (in hours);
(j) fuel consumption at power station' (kWh/kg) and for
vehicles (kml/liter);
(I) number of employees, absenteeism (days) and safari claims
(days/staff)
(1) revenues from electricity sales by category and operating
costs per kWh sold;
(m) number of days of accounts receivable;
(n) debt service coverage and debt-equity ratio;
(o) internally generated funds as 2 of capital investments; and
(p) audited accounts (including special account) and periodic
activity reports.
