TANWZAMA
POWR VI PROJECT
Lover Kihansi Environmental Assessment
Summary
Introduction
4.01        The bulk of Tanzania's energy is derived from woodfuels and
hydropower sources. Woodfuels account for 92S of the energy consumed
with petroleum (72) and electricity (1) accounting for the remainder.
While consumption of modern fuels is relatively low, they play an
important role in Tanzania's economic development.
4.02        Unexploited potential energy sources are substantial and
diverse. In addition to large forest resources, Tanzania has proven coal
deposits estimated at 300 million tons and a significant gas field has
been located offshore near Songo Songo Island, 200 km south of Dar es
Salaam. oil exploration is on-going without significant results. There
is also unexplored geothermal potential. Hydropower potential is
estimated at approximately 4,500 MW of which 330 have been developed.
4.03        Electricity is produced, transmitted and distributed by the
Tanzania Electric Supply Company (TANESCO). Demand for electricity has
been growing at 10X per annum and is expected to continue to grow at 62
per annum until the year 2000 which will require further expansion of
generation, transmission and distribution. The Lower Kihansi
Hydroelectric Project is critical in TANESCO's long-term, least cost
power development plan. TANESCO would be responsible for constructing
and operating the Lower Kihansi power station.
Project Description
4.04        The Kihansi Project area is in a remote and sparsely settled
area of the Uzungwa Highlands roughly 85 km southeast of the city of
Iringa (see Map). The dam and reservoir are located at the top of the
escarpment over 1,000 meters above sea level (masl) while the powerhouse
will be located at the foot of the escarpment at slightly above 300
maal.
4.05        The project     ists of constructing a 3 x 51 MW run-of-
river hydropower facility on the tihansi River, a tributary of the
Rufigi River drainage basin with a catchment area above the proposed dam
site of 590 km2. A 35 m high concrete gravity dam with a crest of 177 m
will create a 30 ha reservoir with a total storage capacity of 1.4
million cubic meters. Effective storage capacity of the reservoir is
nearly 500,000 m3. High water level of the reservoir will be at 1,140
masl with an available drawdown of 3 m.
4.06        An intake, located on the left bank, will have sediment
traps and trashracks and it will direct water into an embedded headrace



- 148 -
Mm 4.10
Page 2 of 9
tunnel of about 1,260 m in length. The steel-lined concrete penstock
will drop the water over 800 a in elevation over a distance of
approximately 3.5 km. After passing through the three turbines, the
water vill be discharged back into the Rihansi River via a 1,240 m
concrete tailrace tunnel.
4.07        While current sediment loads In the Kihansi River would not
exhaust the dead storage in 50 years, a 4 x 4 meter flushing gate is
designed Into the dam in case sediment loads Increase In the catcbment
due to erosion from Intensified agriculture. The combined actions of the
flushing gate, turbines and spiliway will permit a maximum discharge of
400 m3/s, eight times greater than the historic maximum river discharge
of 50 m3l3.
4.08        The powerhouse will be located on the left bank of the river
at the foot of the escarpment which is also the beginning of the
Kilombero Plain at an elevation of 300 masl. The powerhouse vill be
excavated into the base of the escarpment so that only a small part of
the structure will be above ground level. The 0.8 ha switchyard will be
located immediately ad3acent to the powerhouse.
4.09        A 30 km access road will need to be cut up the escarpment
following contours for safety and erosion control to cover the
approximate 4.5 km between the powerhouse and the dam site. It is
assumed that two construction camps will be constructed; one near the
powerhouse on the plain and the second up near the dam and reservoir.
The proposed dimensions of the camps are unknown at the present time.
Proiect Area
4.10        The project site is located at the Uzungwa Escarpment
between the interior highlands above 1,000 masl and a wet lowland
coastal plain of less than 400 masl extending inland from the Indian
Ocean. The Xihansi River rises in gently undulatisg highlands over 2,000
mssl approximately 50 km south of the city of Iringa. The river is part
of a gently flowing dendritic network of tributaries. At the project
site, the gradient increases sharply while passing over the scarp.
4.11        The basin is structured by numerous faults oriented NE-SW
and NNE-SSW. The scarp was formed by these fault groups in an uplifting
in the late Paleozoic to early Mesozoic. No faults can be found within
the limits of site reconnaissance anm boring investigations. Additional
geological surveys and borings are being conducted.
4.12        Geologic investigations and borings at the project site
indicate no serious problems with constructing a 35 m high concrete
gravity dam. Borings for the intake, penstock and tailrace do not
indicate problems for those structures. Investigations at the powerhouse
site indicate a possible fault and if this is verified by the
consultants preparing the detailed design, the powerhouse site can be
shifted to avoid the fault without compromising the project.



Annea 4.1
-                    - 149 -                       Page3 o09
4.13        Owing to the proximity of the Rift Valleys, there is a
history of seismic activity near the project site. The epicenters of 349
earthquakes which occurred in Tanzania between 1910 and 1987 are within
a 1,000 km radius of the project site. As a result of the concern for
dam safety in this seismically active area, the design horizontal
seismic coefficient for the dam is double than what is concidered to be
safe.
4.14        The project area has a tropical savanna climate divided into
a wet and a dry season. Rains generally begin in November and continue
sporadically through May. The weather patterns are strongly influenced
by movements of the intertropical Convergence Zone. The basin has
relatively high rainfall with an annual average of 1,500 mm ranging from
1,00 mm in the western part of the basin to 1,800 mm in the eastern
area. Average annual potential evaporation is 1,919 m. Temperature is
greatly influenced by elevation with maximum temperatures in the
highlands of 26.2 degrees C. and over 30 degrees C. in the lowland
plains. The minimum temperatures show a similar spread when upland
temperatures are compared to ones in the lowland.
4.15        Hydrological data from six gauging stations were used to
calculate river discharge at the project site. These stations have
variable lengths of operation. The most important station has been
operating since 1974 with observations three times per day. Two other
upstream stations have been operating since 1982 and 1984. The oldest of
the six stations has been collecting hydrological data since 1955. The
average annual discharge is 15.87 m3/s with a maximum average of 29.77
in 1969 and a minimum average of 9.42 in 1954. The maximum historic
flood was 50 m3!s and the maximum probable flood is 380 m3is. The flood
discharge applied to dam design is 400 m3/s, eight times the highest
recorded flood discharge.
4.16        Limited suspended solid data were collected during the EA
and these were used to make calculations Qf suspended loads based on
more extensive data collected by PAo on nearby rivers in the Rufigi
drainage. Although trap efficiency of the Lower Mlhansi Dam is nearly
zero, the suspended load calculations assigned a very conservative trap
efficiency of 15. The estimated accumulation of sediments after 50
years derived from the calculations would be 125,000 m3 with a sediment
level at 1,125 masl which is the same level as the sand flushing gate.
4 17        Limited water quality sampling was conducted during the EA
(seven samples between June and September). These data indicate that the
waters are quite acidic and nutrient deficient (oligotrophic). The Ph of
the water is 4.9 and electrical conductivity is low at an average of
17.3 pS/cm. Dissolved oxygen is relatively high ranging between 6.2 and
9.9 mg/l which would be expected from the rapidly moving flow.
4.18        The amount of total suspended solids and the amount of total
residues are relatively high at 120 mg/l and ISC 1/1 respectively.
However, transparency tests show that the water is highly transparent.
The sediments are large grained, mostly mica which would not affect
turbidity or transparency of the water. Very low readings of nitrogen,



- 150 -                     AMm 4.1l2
Psge4 0t9
phosphate and sulfate Indicate that the river does not have high
biological productivity. The water quality of the river is likened to
that of rain water.
4.19        The water quality results are not surprising. The catcbment
area is not highly developed agriculturally and mach of the catchment is
protected by forest reserve. There are very few upstream sources of
pollution and human population densities are low throughout the
catchment.
4.20        As a run-of-river power station, the Lower Kihansi Dam with
nearly zero trap efficiency will not have any severe or long-lasting
downstream effects. During construction, the suspended load will
increase from erosion caused by construction.
This effect will be temporary and it can be reduced In severity by
employing effective erosion control techniques during construction. The
project will not have any effects on the upstream or downstream
groundwater levels. The 4.5 km stretch of the river between the dam
site and the powerhouse will receive reduced flow and perhaps zero flow
during the dry season. The Kihansi Falls will be less spectacular during
the wet season and are likely to dry completely during the dry season.
The falls are attractive, but have not had tourism value because of
their remote and nearly inaccessible location. Tourism in the area is
currently focused on the Selous Game Reserve and this focus is likely to
continue into the future.
4.21        The Kihansi River separates Uzungwa Scarp Forest Reserve and
land used for shifting agriculture. The reserve is a part of the larger
20,500 ha Forest Reserve. On the south bank, land is cultivated using
primLtive slash and burn techniques with hand tools. There is evidence
that better agricultural practices, such as contour planting and
terracing, are being introduced into the area. Due to distance and
difficult roads to markets, farming is mostly for subsistence rather
than cash crops.
4.22        The forest on the north bank is a rich second-growth forest
which has been partially protected for approximately 50 years. During
the EA, 107 species of 38 families of trees were identified. Timber
volume, estimated at 100 tonslha is not high when compared to the 300
tonsIha     found in the average moist tropical forest.  The forest is
considered to have high conservation value because of high species
diversity and for watershed protection.
4.23        Although the forest is legally protected, it is being
lightly exploited for saw timber and fuelwood. During the EA. piles of
pit sawn lumber were noted in various locations, some of which may have
been extracted under government license. Residents of two nearby
villages extract fuelwood from the forests, but certainly well below the
rate of regeneration.
4.24        Wildlife has diminished from earlier times, especially the
larger species, such as elephant and African buffalo. There have not
been any recent sightings of these two species in the project area. The



- 151 -                      Annux 4A10
Pag 5 ofg
highly protected Selous Game Reserve is quite close to the lower part of
the project area and this reserve provides an enormous mount of
protected habitat for wildlife. The Lower Kihansi project will not have
any effects on the reserve.
4.25        The forest provides excellent habitat for many vertebrate
and invertebrate species, many of which are endeamc to the escarpment
forest. During the EA, 124 species of birds belonging to 37 families
were recorded. While the forest is known to contain three rare and
endangered bird species, none were observed during the EA. There are 16
endemic bird species known to occur In the forest reserve. Since less
than 15 ha of forest will be cleared for the reservoir, it is not likely
that the project will have serious adverse effects on wildlife as the
animals would move to a different part of the forest.
4.26        Since the river waters are so deficient In nutrients, there
is not a large, nor varied fish population. Fish caught during the EA
vere small and only a few species were present in the samples. Residents
claim that they do not use the river for fishing. The falls below the
dam site are already an effective barrier to fish migration and the dam
will not constitute any threat to fish migration.-
4.27        During the EA meetings were held at Ukami and Uhafiwa, two
villages within a 10 km radius of the project site. These villages
contain 2,200 and 1,200 residents respectively. Approximately 402 of the
population is less than 12 years old; 502 between 13 and 50 years and
the remaining 101 is over 50 years old. There are three elementary
schools in the two villages. There is a dispensary with a permanent
medical assistant and nurse In Uhafiwa which covers the three villages
of Uhafiwa, Ukami and Ihimbo. There is a mission hospital equipped for
In-patient care and minor surgery at Usokami village, 40 km on the route
to Iringa.
4.28        In the villages, people were told the basic facts about the
project. Many were aware of the project and enthusiastically supported
the concept. Access is very difficult to and from the villages,
consisting of one very rough road/track to Iringa, 80 km distant. It
takes more than three hours by car or truck to make the trip between the
villages and Iringa. Bus service is available for the first 40 km from
Iringa. The only other means of access is a foot path down the
escarpment to the road connecting the villages of Ifakara and Chita. It
takes nearly one day to make the trip down the steep and treacherous
path and the return trip is much more difficult. Therefore. the project
area residents see the project as a means to improve access to markets
as well as offering possible employment directly or indirectly related
to the project.
4.29        Tanzania has developed the ten cell system for communicating
between government and the rural populations. Ten households send one
representative to the basic cell to discuss the everyday issues relating
to rural living. The elected leader of this cell reports the discussions
and consensus to the village chairperson who in turn raises the issues
with the village council. The results of the village council meetings



Annexc 4.12
- 152 -                  Page 6 of 9
are reported to the District Commissioner who is responsible for
coordinating with the national government. Actions taken at the
na.ional level are reported to rural populations in the reverse order,
from District Commissioner to village council to cell leader. The
residents of Ukami and Uhafiwa expressed satisfaction with the system
and agreed to use this system of communication to continue their
participation in the Lower Kihansi project.
4.30        Only two dwellings which are only temporarily occupied will
need to be removed for the project construction. These houses are
totally without services of electricity, water supply and sanitation.
The households had been previously relocated to Uhafiva and Ukami In
1974 under a government project to strengthen rural communities. For
unknown reasons, the families decided to return to their homes near the
Lower Kihansi dam site.
4.31        The most common diseases reported by the dispensary at
Uhafiwa are respiratory diseases including pneumonia, malaria, diarrheal
diseases and helminthiasis (unspecified). The river does not provide
conducive habitat for mosquitos or the snail hosts of bilhar:ia. There
were two cases of bilharzia reported in Uhafiwa in 1986, but the source
of the infection is unknown. It is likely that residents of the villages
are exposed to malaria and bilharzia when they visit the lowland plains
which are covered by wetlands that are excellent habitat for mosquitos
and snails. The reservoir, being so small and with very short retention
time, will not be suitable habitat for either snails or mosquitos. The
reservoir will not be used by people for recreation, bathing or washing,
thus there will be no increased human exposure to water-borne or water-
related diseases as a result of the project.
4.32        There have been no reported cases of AIDS from the
dispensary and it is possible that the disease has not yet spread to the
project area. AIDS in rural areas is more commonly associated with
transport routes, possibly being spread by truck drivers. Since the
project area is so remote, the probability of the introduction of AIDS
into the population is less than it would be for many other rural areas.
Construction of the dam and powerhouse will create an influx of labor
and associated commercial activities into the project area. This influx
of people, many from urban areas, definitely Increases the possibility
of AIDS transmission. Labor camps at large construction projects
attract young and sexually active people who seek to take advantage of
the steady Income earned by the project labor force. These are ideal
conditions for the spread of AIDS.
4.33        During the discussions with residents, the EA team was
informed of the existence of a pre-colonial iron smelter located
upstream of the proposed dam site. The team did not visit the site, but
residents claimed that it was distant from the site and that it would
not be affected by the project.
4.34        There are no national parks in the vicinity of the project
site. Part of the Uzungwa Mountains are plauned to be designated as a
national park in the future but the proposed area is more than 100 km



- 153 -
Anw&AQ
Pgge 7 of 9
from the project area. The Selous Game Reserve is close to the proposed
powerhouse, but will not be affected by the construction or operation of
the plant. Kibasira Game Controlled Area is between the powerhouse site
and Selous Game Reserve. The project effects oan the game area would be
temporary disturbances due to noise.
Probable Proiect Imoacts
4.35        Approximately 30 ha of land will be lost for the reservoir
and another 15 to 50 ha will be used for access road construction and
the construction camps. Only 15 ha of the land lost is in natural
forest. Two temporarily occupied dwellings will be removed as a result
of project implementation.
4.36        The project will not increase the incidence of water-related
diseases because the reservoir will not provide conducive habitat for
mosquitos or snail hosts of bilharzia. The major health threat of the
project is the possible introduction of AIDS into the area by the labor
force which will include urban workers.
4.37        Wildlife habitat will be reduced by 15-20 ha. It is expected
that wildlife will move to adjacent areas of similar habitat. Wildlife
could be reduced through harassment and illegal hunting by the labor
force if the contractor does not enforce strict regulations to protect
wildlife. Major fish movements will not be altered as a result of the
project because fish movements have been historically blocked by the
Kihansi Falls. Fishing in the downstream wetlands will not be affected
by th. run-of-river project.
4.38         Water quality will not be directly altered by the project
except some increase in downstream sediment loads resulting from
construction activities. This would be a temporary condition and will
not create significant damage to the downstream wetlands. The project
may cause indirect deterioration of water quality by encouraging human
inmigration to the area through the improved access. This inmigration
would intensify land use, mostly for agriculture. This increased use
would increase erosion and sediment loads in the river. The intensified
agriculture may stimulate widespread use of agricultural chemicals which
would run-off into the river. Waterweeds are not expected to alter water
quality or jeopardize plant operation since the reservoir will not be
conducive to waterweed proliferation.
4.39        The project will create a large amount of excavated material
and environmentally sound disposal techniques will be required. It is
possible that the material could be used in road rehabilitation or
improvement. The material may also be used to help In stabilizing the
access road.
Analysis of Alternatives
4.40        Several power generating alternatives have been evaluated in
two national power studies. Each plan considers hydropower to be the
least cost option of expansion of the Tanzania power grid, since the



- 154 -
MuAi 4.10
Page a of 9
country has not exploited reserves of fossil fuels. Ezploitation of the
Songo Songo Island gas field for power generation was studied but was
not found feasible.
4.41        Bydropower is usually a non-polluting power source and under
most conditions it is considered to be environmentally preferable to
fossil-fueled power generation. With the current low per capita energy
consumption In Tanzania, energy conservation is not a viable option for
meeting Tanzania's developmeat needs in the medium- to long-term.
TANESCO ls making efforts to reduce system losses and is vigorously
pursuing a program to have a fully metered system.
4.42        Alternate hydropower sites were evaluated, including another
site on the Ribansi River. The Lower Kihansi site was selected for the
next generating station for Tanzania on the basis of economic and
engineering studies. The Lower Ktihansi site is also the most
environmentally preferable site because of the remote nature of the site
and the small size of the reservoir. While other hydropower sites in
Tanzania may also pose as few environmental risks as the Lower Kihansi
project, the EA demonstrates that Lower Kihansi is an acceptable site
and that it does not pose serious environmental problems.
Recosmended Mitiaation Measures
4.43        TANESCO should establish an in-house environmental unit to
be based at Kibansi during construction. This unit would be responsible
for environmental supervision and monitoring at the site during
construction. The unit would also provide liaison between the
construction contractor and the local populations. The unit would also
be responsible for implementation of mitigation measures for the
project.
4.44        TANESCO should prepare strict environmental protection
clauses for bidding documents for the contractors. These clauses should
require that the contractor enforce regulations to protect wildlife
through strict sanctions regarding illegal hunting and harassment of
wildlife. These clauses should also insist on strict erosion control
practices and require that the contractor respond quickly to any erosion
detected by the environmental supervision unit rather than having the
contractor wait until the end of construction to carry out restoration.
The contracts should also require the contractor to maintain access
roads so that they do not contribute to excessive erosion. If erosion
does occur, the contractor must control it quickly or face financial
penalties. The contract should also specify that the contractor will
develop methods to control all wastes including sanitary and
construction wastes.
4.45        TANESCO should make sure that the area to be inundated will
be completely cleared of all woody vegetation. This vegetation should be
removed from.the reservoir area.
4.46        The contractor should be required to protec   the health of
all employees with medical screening as a pre-requisite to employment



- 155 -                   ABDSa410
Page 9 of 9
including EIV screening. The contractor should be required to maintain
adequate health services for all employees and their families Including
the unskilled laborers. The contractor should be required to engage a
local NGO or private company to conduct a health education program
including materials on the transmissioA of the AIDS virus. This program
should cover workers, famillies and the residents of the local villages.
4.47        At the completion of construction, the contractor should be
required to restore all borrow areas, quarries, abandoned camp
facilities and other landscape disruptions to an acceptable stabilized
condition that will revert to natural conditions if no further
construction or disruption takes place.
4.48        TANESCO must Insure that the two households are equitably
relocated and compensated for all losses. The company should also
devise a system to quickly compensate other land holders who will lose
cropland or economic trees as a result of the ih4ansi project. It !s
preferable that the compensation be made directly between the power
company and the affected land holders rather than pursue a eompensation
mechanism which involves various levels of government agencies.
4.49        TANESCO should continue to use the Ten Cell system for
communication with the local populations.
Monitoring
4.50        The in-house unit would be responsible for designing and
implementing a realistic monitoring plan. If this unit is based at the
project site during construction, it will insure effective monitoring
and environmental supervision.

LOR RKIWESI HYDROELECTIC SCHEME
Terms of Reference for p!anel of Exoerts
1.          A Panel of Experts (POE) will be appointed by TANESCO, to
undertake periodic, comprehensive and independent review of the design and
construction of the dam and appurtenentmstructures. The objectives of the
review are safe, economic, state of the art designs and efficient and
expeditious construction.
Organlzation and Membershig
2.          The Panel shall contain four permanent members with wide,
specialised experience in the following fields:
- Engineering Geology
- Rock Mechanics and the Design of Underground Works
- Planning and Design of Dams and Hydropower Facilities
(Generalist)
- Construction of Dams, Tunnels and Hydropower Facilities
3.          At appropriate times during the design and construction of the
Project, the Panel will be enlarged as necessary by the addition of
specialists in other fields, such as hydrology, sedimentology, hydraulics,
high head turbines and in any other area, where the Chairman of the Panel, in
consultation with TANESCO, considers it advisable.
4.          A Chairman will be appointed by TABESCO to coordinate
communications of the POE, to call and chair its meetings, to ensure the
membershIp's activity and to provide balance to its reviews and
recommendations.
S.          The composition of the Panel, and the areas of expertise which it
covers may be varied during the course of the Project as considered
appropriate by TANESCO in consultation with the Chairman.
eetsincs and Schedules
6.          The Panel will convene at regular intervals to review the status
of work in progress. The frequency of meetings and their timing will be
adjusted to conform with the schedule of works in progress, but the time
between meetings shall not normally exceed six months. At each meeting the
scheduled dates for the next mreting and tentative timing for the subsequent
meeting will normally be fixed, to enable Panel member to arrange their
individual schedules. Extraordinary meetings of the Panel may be called in
critical situations, and the services of individual members may be solicited
between meetings as considered necessary or desirable by TANESCO with copies
of their input being sent to other Panel members.
7.          The Panel will pr pare and submit written proceedings of the
meetings (regular or extraordinary), including recommendations, within three
weeks of the meetings, and in case of emergency will prepare technical
memoranda or aide memoires during the meetings. A preliminary report will be
prepareed before the adjournment of each meeting. All reports will be
submitted to TANESCO and through them to the World Bank.
Seope of Reviewg
S.   The subjects on which the Panel are expected to comment are listed
below. The Panel should also comment on any other matter which it perceives
to be important to the successful completion and operation of the project.

powerhouse and switchyard.

EVALATION OF ECONOMIC BENEFITS
1.           TANESCO's 1992 tariffs do not fully represent the economic benefits
of the additional electricity to be provided through the investment program since they
appear to be below consumers' willingness-to-pay for electricity supply, particularly
for the residential consumers. The value of the benefits associated with the additional
electricity consumption has been estimated based on the evaluation of revenue benefits
to TANESCO of the incremental electricity sales and an esfimation of the consumer
surplus enjoyed by electricity consumers.
2.           Consumers valuation of electricity is calculated according to an
approximated demand function for each of the four major consumer categories:
residential, small commercial, small industrial and energy intensive commercial and
Industrial. The demand fimctions have been estimated using two observations: (1) the
observed willingness-pay for alternative energy sources such as keosene for
lighting and electricity from captive diesel units; and (2) the observed cost of using
electricity, based on TANESCO's 1992 marginal unit energy rates.
3.           Benefits from increased comfort (such as improved lighting) and
increased productivity accruing from the provision of grid electricity are not taken
into account.
4.           The following tables summarize the benefit calculations.

TAAM EL13&TR sMY COMPANY LIMr1=
s rVA    OF POCY
1.        hMe statemet as amended ftom time to time, defines the policies which shall apply
to the activities and operaions of the Tanzania Electric Supply Company Limited hereinafter
referred to as the 'Company.'
2.         The ComPany is an independen institution established as a public limited Company
under the Company Ordinance to generate, transmit, distribute and supply electricity within
Tanzania and other countries in the region. It operates in accordance with the rules and
regulations provided for under the Electricity Ordinance of October 1931. In pursuance of these
objectives the Company acquires, maintains and operates its facilities, as far as practicable, a; the
lowest cost and with maximum economy and efficiency. The Company conducts its business on
commercial principles and performs its finctions in such a manner to ensure that, taking one year
with another, its income is sufficient to meet its expenditures, properly chargeable to revenue
account and its net operating income is adecuate to secure an annual return on the value of the net
fixed assets in operations as revalued from twie to time by the company, of not less than 10
percent per anmum.
3.         To finance its activities, the Company raises nec-sary funds through loans from the
government and other organizations, through normal business activities and issue of stock.
4.         It is part of the Company's objectives to increase efficiency, promote competition
and ensure the adequate supply of electricity for Tanzania. 'he Government is studying
privatzation options. This inludes the development of a sound legal and regulatory famework
to ensre proper operation of both public and private enterprises in the power sector.

relating to the generation and supply of electricity within Tanzania. It aims to keep the cost of
svices it provides as low as poss;5le while metng its obligations outlined in paragraph 2 above
to cover its development, operatng and financial costs, make adequate provision against bad debts
and other losses, build up reserves adequate to maintain its financial integrity and soundness as
well as earn reasonable retmun on the value of its net fixed assets.

Pg,ble Taret  So   for the Dwr Socto
Ihe following pages describe various structural options for introducing private sector
involvement in the power sector in Tanzania. Each description is accompanied by a list of
perceived advantages and disadvantages.
Each of the options described is a possible target struture for the power sector and would
require special transitional arrangements to transform the sector from its present structure to the
one desired. Some of the major issues that must be dealt with in such a transition are:
3 Thne Impact of the changes on employment: Although any new entities formed would
absorb many of the present employees, special provision mst be made to retran and
fid useful employment for those not immediately required.
*    Phasing-in of tariff dcanges: Tariff changes may have to be coordinated with
imparovements in the reliability and quality of electricity supply to minimize
opposition by customers.
*    Legal framework: The legal framework governing the sector must be revised to
accommodate the proposed structure and provide a mechanism for seting disputes
between all interested parties.
*    Reguatory f*amework: A reguatory framework appropriate for supporting the
selected option must be finnly in place when the new structure is introduced. This
fiamework must address price, safety, quality of supply and environmental issues.
*    Valuation and transfer of assets and debt: The asses of any entity to be restrctured
must be valued commercily. Rational decisions must be made regarding the
transfer of the assets, and the responsibility for debt service.
*    Planning of the power sector: For the decentralized options, it is necessary to form
an organization to plan the sector. Ihe activiies of this orgarization must not
interfere with campetition in the sector. Rather, the organization should seek to
guide rational development by collecting, analyzing and providing information on
rational generation and transmission expansion strategies. An important part of this
organization's work would be identifying sites and evaluating potential hydroelectric
resources. Also, it would have to evaluate ecological, human settlement, and safety
issues.
*    Managing the transiton: Special adminis ve arrangements, by the Government
and the Utility, would be required for managing the transition and resolving
problems as they arise.

:)   A distribution company buys from geneatng and/or ansmission companies two products:
energy and power at prices set by those companies and sells them at higher prices. The
difference is called "distrnbution added value*.
2)   Ihe difference between energy prices is equal to distributon systm  energy losses.
3)   Tem difference between power prices is equal to distnbution system power losses plus a
term K which includes investm    and operation costs.
4)   K can be broken down in the following main items:
A:   equipment prices
B:   labour costs
C:   copper prices
D: Exchange rate and custom duties
Therefore: K-A + B +C + D
5)   Ihe values of A,B,C and D depend on several factors like:
consumer density, service quality level, return on investment, consumers activities
(domestic, industrial, commercial, etc.)
6)   The most practical way to regulate distribudon prices is to deine an Ideal distribution
company which is deemed adeuate to serve the area where the real disibtion company is
operating. The above defined pameters are then calcated. A reference rate of rtumn
should be chosen, say 10% (as in Chile), and a maximum losses level stated. (8 to 10%).
7)   The distibuin added values for this ideal company can now be calculated and taben   a
basis for the using of the regulated prices for the real distribution company.

Am 7.2
Page 7 of 7
8)   If the company's efficienc is better than the ideal company's the reward will be a rate of
rtu  greater than 10%. On the conuay, If the company's efficiency is below the idea
company's, the punihment will be a rate of return less than 10%.
9)   The above described method which Is being used in Chile since 1982 has two Importan
features:
a]   Competition:     the distribution company in spite of being a natural monopoly is
competing against an ideal company.
bl   Rate of    the ve of return i not pa teed but neither  it limited.

10) Indexation is taken care of as follows:
A,B,C and D parameters are multiplied by the following ratios:
K= Alk + BL +CIcg +D U

Where:
1w: Wholesale prices nex (given by the Bureau of Saistics)
!L:  Wages index (iven by the Ministy of Labour)
Icw: Price of copper (given by the London Metal Exchange)
If:  Rate of exchange and cusom duties (given by the Central Bank)
Wheneve the combined effe  of these 4 indexes caues K to increase by more than 10%,
the rates are immel y adjusted by the companies themselves, without having to obtain
the goverment's permission.

Tanzania Electric Supply Company Limited (TANESCO) aims to establish a Corporate
Planning Division which would coordinate and assist in preparation of Corporate Plans (business plans
for the company) annually, monitor actual results and identify reasons/causes for variations between
planned targets and actual results. To reflect and report on the performance of the institution the
Corporate Planning Division will prepare periodic management reports on a regular basis. Depending
on the nature of the activities covered by the reports, the management reports will be prepared on a
monthly, quarterly, half-yearly and yearly basis. The main Stewardship Report for TANESCO's senior
management and its Board of Directors will be prepared on a moiathly basis.
The Corporate Plamning Mager will have the overall responsibility for coordinating all
planning activities within the company. The Corporate Planning Manager will be assisted by an assistant
manager and a team of planning and senior planning officers and support staff. Hetsh* turn will report
to the Finance Director.
Work Pro&ra
The Corporate Planning Manager will focus his/her attention on the following activities:
(a) review TANESCO's business objectives and activities to identify disftinct operating,
functional and work units which will be assigned the responsibility for preparing business
plans for their respective work units;
(b)   prepare an outine of the Annual Corporate Plan and review it with TANESCO's
managers at different levels to reach a consensus on the activities to be covered by the
plan and its format;
(c)   prepare detailed instructions for preparing the annual plan, design forms, schedules,
charts etc. to be completed by each planning unit, prepare a timetable for completion of
the planning activities at various levels of the organization until their consolidation into
a draft Annual Corporate Plan;
(d)   consolidate inputs of all planning work units into one Annual Corporate Plan;

IANZ&AJA
Power VI Project
Twems of Refeenc
Cronsulpng Servit Stuf Option for the Insttutonal em enot
or               nd Asslst In ImoIementing the fareed O-tTon
Objectives:
1.    The objectives of the assignment are to:
(a)   list the advantages and disadvantages of various possible institutional
structures to improve efficiency and expand investment in Tanzania's
power sector;
(b)   recommend a structure including the enabling legal framework,
regulatory framework and transitional arrangements that will result in
the ptovision of an adequate supply of good quality electrical service
atleast cost; and
(b)   once the Government has agreed on the structure, help it implement
the necessary changes.
2.    The Tanzania Electric Supply Company Ltd. (TANESCO) builds and operates the
public power system in mainland Tanzania. TANESCO was incorporated ir November 1931
as a private company called the Tanganyika Electric Supply Company. The Government
bought the company in 1964 and established it as a public limited company under the
Company Ordinance Act. In 1968 the company's name was changed to the Tanzania Electric
Supply Company. Both the Company Ordinanc Act, and the Electricity Ordinance Act of
October 1931 govern its operatons.
3.    TANESCO is managed by a Board of Directors through the Managing Director. The
Govemment, the sole shareholder, nominates the Directors, including the Managing Director,
who, according to the Artidles of Association, cannot be less than two or more than ten. At
preset, the Board consists of the Chairman who is also the Chairman of the Paraststal Sector
Refom Commission, the Managing Director, and nine other Directors that represent most
interest groups in Tanzanian society including the workers.
4.    As a public limited company, TANESCO is intended to operate independenty. In
practice, however, it has to seek Government approval on major investments. The Board of
Directors has the authority to increase tariffs by up to 5% semiaually, and the Ministry of
Water, Energy, and Mieras to which TANESCO reports can raise the tariffs by a further
lO% smannmualy. Increases more an 15% require Cabinet approval.



- 204 -                         Annex 8.8
Page 2 of 4
S.    TANESCO generates and sells about 95% of the power consumed in The country In
an inrconnected system with an Installed capacity of about 474 MW and several small
isolated symems. The Zanzibar State Fuel and Power Company sells the electricity consumed
on Zanzibar and Pemba (about 8% of the total consumed). A submarine cable from the
manland supplies Zanzibar's electricity. Diesel engines generate Pemba's electricity. The
Mistry of Water, Energy and Minerals, in consultation with Tanesco, controls the
generation of electricity by private individuals through a licensing mechanism.
6.    Most of the foreip currency financing for the sector comes from concessionary
multlatral and bilateral loans or grants.
Bldoe for Chang
7.    Although TANESCO is considered one of the most efficient Government owned
companies, there Is scope for improvement in the efficiency of the operation. Also, if
TANESCO had better access to foreign curraency it would be able to respond to expansion and
maintenance needs faster. Moreover, a regulatory framework that responds to commercW
needs and is independent of the political process would permit the supply of higher quality
serce.
The main objecdves of chage are:
(a)   to promote sustained efficiency by introducing competMion, where
possible, aid incentives into the operation of the sector;
(b) to build a structure that promotes full commercial operation of the
power sector and allows the Governmeent to concentrate on policy and
operate at arm's length from tariff setting;
(c)   to attract private capital and so, reduce the burden on public finances;
and
(d)   to improve the access of the population to electricity service and
provide sufficient elecrity of the proper quality to sustain strong
economic growth.
It Is necessry to Introduce structura reforms and institonal arrangements that will be both
acceptable and practical and in which prices are controlled either by competition or where this
is not possible by fair and transparent regutation. The transition from the present stucture to
the proposed structre must be carefully planned to minze the impact on the mangent
and staff of the present organizations, and on the provision of electricity service during this
petiod.
S.    lEo cons_t should:



- 205 -                          Amex 8.8
Page 3 of 4
(a)   thoroughly investigate the efficiency of the present sector
arrangements with respect to the objectives in para. 7, including the
sector's legal and regulatory framework, management of the utility,
fiacing of the sector, labor laws, labor union roles and behavior,
regional differences of access to service etc.;
(b)   thoroughly document factors that would affect private investment in
the sector, e.g. present and forecast economic performance, supply of
foreign exchange,customs and taxation(the intention is to take this
information as given and not to recommend changes to policies
generated outside of the power sector);
(c)    suggest several alternative sets of arrangements for the sector that
would help meet the objectives of para. 7 above, including in the
discussion of each a full listing of its advantages and disadvantages
and the associated transitional arrangement;
(d)    discuss and recommend approaches to deal with different supply costs
in parts of the country, i.e. uniform versus non-uniform tariffs ( the
Consultants should take into account work being done in a separate
tariff study);
(e)    discuss and recommend approaches to deal with low income
customers; and
(f)   recommend to the Government the best arrangement for the sector
taking into account the advantages, disadvantages and any constraints.
For new options in (c) above, the Consultants should consider various combinations of.
(1)    setting up the distribution zones as companies;
(ii)   setting up a separate transmission and system control company;
(iii) setting up separate generating companies.
The Consultants should consider these combinations with various ownership and management
options, taking into account present and forecast conditions in the country.
9.    In selecting regulatory systems, the Consutants should carefully consider the poteni
usefulne  of other institutions in the country. In particular they should assess whether an
bnde t regulatory instition would be feasible, and should restrict the role of regulation
to those areas of the system for which efective competition is not likely to occur. The
Consultants should oudine and evaluate the merits, In the TanzaWnan context, of several
rgulaoy models, from international experience, for example, a quasi-legal public hearing
approach, a ontact approach and administrative approaches. lhe consultants should then
recommend an approach, explaining its particular suitability to Tanzania.



- 206 -                        Amex 8.8
Page 4 of 4
10.   If the Consultants find that privatization of a part of the sector would be
advantageous, but is not immediately possible because of perceived county risks, the
Consultants should evaluate the desirability of an interim solution using performance based
contas.
11.   lte reco     ion to the government should be in the form of a detailed plan that
shows the argemen tfor introducing competition into the sector, regulation of the sector,
planning of sector expansion and ownership and management of the utilities, expected sources
of financing, and any enabling legislation needed. Once the plan has been refined to consider
any concerns resulting from its discussion with IDA, cofinanciers and the government, the
Consultants should prepare a detailed implementation schedule, in consultation with the parties
concerned, and help the goverment implement it.
12.   Ihe Consultans should produce regular concise progress reports and a final report
tw recommends the sector stucture. The Consultants should arrange a seminar to present
and explain the study process and the recommended sector structure to the ierested parties
including the Government, IDA and cofinanciers. The study leading to a recommendation is
expected to be completed In six months to be ready for discussion during the proposed Interim
review of the project.
